910 <section id="_1">

911

912 <h2>

913General Information About Childhood Liver Cancer</h2>

914

915 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

916<p id="_379" tabindex="-1">

917Liver cancer is a rare malignancy in children and adolescents and is divided into the following two

918major histological subgroups:</p>

919<div class="pdq-content-list">

920<ul id="_578">

921<li>

922<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_567">

923Hepatoblastoma</a>

924.<div class="pdq-content-list">

925<ul id="_1849">

926<li>

927<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_517">

928Well-differentiated fetal (pure fetal) histology</a>

929.</li>

930<li>

931Mixed epithelial and fetal histology.</li>

932<li>

933<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_493">

934Small cell undifferentiated histology hepatoblastoma and rhabdoid tumors of the liver</a>

935.<div class="pdq-content-list">

936<ul id="_1988">

937<li>

938Small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma (<em class="gene-name">

939SMARCB1</em>

940 positive).</li>

941<li>

942Rhabdoid tumor of liver (<em class="gene-name">

943SMARCB1</em>

944 negative).</li>

945</ul>

946</div>

947</li>

948</ul>

949</div>

950</li>

951<li>

952<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_162">

953Hepatocellular carcinoma</a>

954.</li>

955</ul>

956</div>

957<p id="_579" tabindex="-1">

958Other, less common histologies include the following:</p>

959<div class="pdq-content-list">

960<ul id="_580">

961<li>

962<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_355">

963Undifferentiated embryonal sarcoma of the liver</a>

964.</li>

965<li>

966<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_357">

967Infantile choriocarcinoma of the liver</a>

968.</li>

969<li>

970<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_934">

971Vascular liver tumors</a>

972.</li>

973<li>

974Biliary rhabdomyosarcoma. For more information, see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/rhabdomyosarcoma-treatment-pdq">

975Childhood Rhabdomyosarcoma Treatment</a>

976.</li>

977</ul>

978</div>

979</div>

980

981 </section>

982

983

984

985 <section id="_608">

986

987 <h2>

988Cellular Classification of Childhood Liver Cancer</h2>

989

990 <div id="_section_608" class="pdq-sections">

991<p id="_609" tabindex="-1">

992Liver tumors are rare in children. A diagnosis may be challenging, in part because of the lack of consensus regarding a classification system. Systematic central histopathological review of these tumors

993performed as part of pediatric collaborative therapeutic protocols has allowed the identification of

994histological subtypes with distinct clinical associations. As a result, histopathology has been incorporated within

995the Children&#8217;s Oncology Group (COG) protocols and, in the United States, as a risk-stratification parameter

996used for patient management. </p>

997<p id="_757" tabindex="-1">

998The COG Liver Tumor Committee sponsored an International Pathology

999Symposium in 2011 to discuss the histopathology and classification of pediatric liver tumors (hepatoblastoma, in particular) and develop an International Pediatric Liver Tumors Consensus

1000Classification that would be required for international collaborative projects. The results of this international classification for pediatric liver tumors have been published.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

10011</a>

1002] This standardized, clinically meaningful classification will allow the

1003integration of new biological parameters and tumor genetics within a common pathologic language to help improve future patient management and outcomes.

1004</p>

1005<p id="_610" tabindex="-1">

1006For information about the histology of each childhood liver cancer subtype, see the following sections:</p>

1007<div class="pdq-content-list">

1008<ul id="_611">

1009<li>

1010<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_585">

1011Hepatoblastoma</a>

1012.</li>

1013<li>

1014<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_606">

1015Hepatocellular carcinoma</a>

1016.</li>

1017<li>

1018<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_632">

1019Undifferentiated embryonal sarcoma of the liver</a>

1020.</li>

1021<li>

1022<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_1986">

1023Infantile choriocarcinoma of the liver</a>

1024.</li>

1025<li>

1026<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_934">

1027Vascular liver tumors</a>

1028.</li>

1029</ul>

1030</div>

1031<h6 do-not-show="toc">

1032References</h6>

1033<ol>

1034<li id="section_2.1">

1035L&#243;pez-Terrada D, Alaggio R, de D&#225;vila MT, et al.: Towards an international pediatric liver tumor consensus classification: proceedings of the Los Angeles COG liver tumors symposium. Mod Pathol 27 (3): 472-91, 2014.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24008558&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=24008558&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1036[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

1037</li>

1038</ol>

1039</div>

1040

1041 </section>

1042

1043

1044

1045 <section id="_10">

1046

1047 <h2>

1048Tumor Stratification by Imaging and Evans Surgical Staging for Childhood Liver Cancer</h2>

1049

1050 <div id="_section_10" class="pdq-sections">

1051<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1052<h6>

1053In This Section</h6>

1054<ul>

1055<li>

1056<a href="#_1831_toc">

1057Tumor Stratification by Imaging</a>

1058</li>

1059<li>

1060<a href="#_728_toc">

1061PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group Definitions<ul>

1062<li>

1063<a href="#_2408_toc">

1064PRETEXT</a>

1065</li>

1066<li>

1067<a href="#_2410_toc">

1068POSTTEXT</a>

1069</li>

1070</ul>

1071</a>

1072</li>

1073<li>

1074<a href="#_11_toc">

1075Evans Surgical Staging for Childhood Liver Cancer (Historical)</a>

1076</li>

1077</ul>

1078</nav>

1079<p id="_726" tabindex="-1">

1080Historically, the four major study groups&#8212;International Childhood Liver Tumors Strategy Group (previously known as Soci&#233;t&#233;

1081Internationale d&#8217;Oncologie P&#233;diatrique&#8211;Epithelial Liver Tumor Study Group [SIOPEL]), Children's Oncology Group (COG), Gesellschaft f&#252;r P&#228;diatrische Onkologie und H&#228;matologie (Society for Paediatric Oncology and Haematology), and

1082Japanese Study Group for Pediatric Liver Tumors&#8212;have had disparate risk stratification categories, making it difficult to compare outcomes across continents. All groups are now using the PRE-Treatment EXTent of tumor (PRETEXT) grouping system as part of the risk stratification.</p>

1083<section id="_1831">

1084<h3 id="_1831_toc">

1085Tumor Stratification by Imaging</h3>

1086<p id="_1832" tabindex="-1">

1087The main treatment goal for patients with liver cancer is surgical extirpation of the primary tumor. Therefore, the risk grouping depends heavily on factors determined by imaging that are related to safe surgical resection of the tumor, as well as the PRETEXT grouping. These imaging findings include the section or sections of the liver that are involved with the tumor and additional findings, termed <em>

1088annotation factors</em>

1089, that impact surgical decision making and prognosis.</p>

1090<p id="_1833" tabindex="-1">

1091Risk stratification of children with hepatoblastoma depends on the use of high-quality, cross-sectional imaging. Three-phase computed tomography scanning (noncontrast, arterial, and venous) or magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) with contrast agents are used for imaging. MRI with gadoxetate disodium, a gadolinium-based agent that is preferentially taken up and excreted by hepatocytes, is being used with increased frequency and may improve detection of multifocal disease.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

10921</a>

1093]</p>

1094</section>

1095<section id="_728">

1096<h3 id="_728_toc">

1097PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group Definitions</h3>

1098<p id="_1834" tabindex="-1">

1099The imaging grouping systems used to radiologically define the extent of liver involvement by the tumor are designated as the following:</p>

1100<div class="pdq-content-list">

1101<ul id="_1835">

1102<li>

1103<strong>

1104PRETEXT (PRE-Treatment EXTent of disease):</strong>

1105 The extent of liver involvement is defined <strong>

1106before</strong>

1107 therapy.</li>

1108<li>

1109<strong>

1110POSTTEXT (POST-Treatment EXTent of disease):</strong>

1111 The extent of liver involvement is defined <strong>

1112in response to</strong>

1113 therapy.</li>

1114</ul>

1115</div>

1116<section id="_2408">

1117<h4 id="_2408_toc">

1118PRETEXT</h4>

1119</section>

1120<p id="_617" tabindex="-1">

1121PRETEXT is used by the major multicenter trial groups as a central component of risk stratification schemes that define treatment of hepatoblastoma. PRETEXT is based on the Couinaud eight-segment anatomic structure of the liver using cross-sectional imaging. </p>

1122<p id="_2409" tabindex="-1">

1123The PRETEXT system divides the liver into four parts, called sections. The left lobe of the liver consists of a lateral section (Couinaud segments I, II, and III) and a medial section (segment IV), whereas the right lobe consists of an anterior section (segments V and VIII) and a posterior section (segments VI and VII) (see <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_2430">

1124Figure 1</a>

1125). PRETEXT groups were devised by the SIOPEL for their first trial, SIOPEL-1,[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

11262</a>

1127] and revised for the SIOPEL-3 trial in 2007.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

11283</a>

1129]</p>

1130<figure id="figure_2430" class="image-center">

1131<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/810953.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

1132Enlarge</a>

1133<img id="_2430" alt="Drawing showing 4 sections of the liver: the right posterior section, the right anterior section, the left medial section, and the left lateral section. Also shown are 8 segments (I-VIII), each corresponding to a specific section of the liver. The boundaries of each section are separated by the right hepatic vein, middle hepatic vein, and left hepatic vein. The vena cava and portal vein are also shown." title="Drawing showing 4 sections of the liver: the right posterior section, the right anterior section, the left medial section, and the left lateral section. Also shown are 8 segments (I-VIII), each corresponding to a specific section of the liver. The boundaries of each section are separated by the right hepatic vein, middle hepatic vein, and left hepatic vein. The vena cava and portal vein are also shown." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/810953-750.jpg">

1134<figcaption class="caption-container">

1135Figure 1. The liver is divided into four sections: the right posterior section, the right anterior section, the left medial section, and the left lateral section. Each section of the liver is further divided into segments: segments VI and VII make up the right posterior section, segments V and VIII make up the right anterior section, segment IV makes up the left medial section, and segments II and III make up the left lateral section. Segment I is found deep in the left side of the liver, in front of the inferior vena cava and behind the right, middle, and left hepatic veins.</figcaption>

1136</figure>

1137<p id="_619" tabindex="-1">

1138PRETEXT group assignment I, II, III, or IV is determined by the number of uninvolved sections of the liver. PRETEXT is further described by annotation factors. Annotation factors include findings that are important for surgical management and evidence of tumor extension beyond the hepatic parenchyma of the major sections, including metastatic disease. For detailed descriptions of the PRETEXT groups, see <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_696">

1139Table 1</a>

1140. For descriptions of the annotation factors, see <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_1907">

1141Table 2</a>

1142.</p>

1143<p id="_763" tabindex="-1">

1144Annotation factors identify the extent of tumor involvement of the major vessels and its effect on venous inflow and outflow, which is critical knowledge for the surgeon and can affect surgical outcomes. There were differences in the definitions of gross vascular involvement used by the COG and major liver surgery centers in the United States compared with SIOPEL definitions used in Europe. These differences have been resolved, and the new definitions are being used in an international trial that began in 2018.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

11454</a>

1146]</p>

1147<p id="_719" tabindex="-1">

1148Although PRETEXT can be used to predict tumor resectability, there are limitations. The distinction between real invasion beyond the anatomical border of a given hepatic section and the compression and displacement by the tumor can be difficult, especially at diagnosis. Additionally, it can be difficult to distinguish between vessel encroachment and involvement, particularly if imaging is inadequate. The PRETEXT group assignment has a moderate degree of interobserver variability. In a report published in 2005 using data from the SIOPEL-1 study, the preoperative PRETEXT group aligned with postoperative pathological findings only 51% of the time, with overstaging in 37% of patients and understaging in 12% of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

11495</a>

1150] </p>

1151<p id="_764" tabindex="-1">

1152Because distinguishing PRETEXT group assignment is difficult, central review of imaging is critical and is generally performed in all major clinical trials. For patients not enrolled in clinical trials, expert radiological review should be considered in questionable cases in which the PRETEXT group assignment affects choice of treatment. </p>

1588<section id="_2410">

1589<h4 id="_2410_toc">

1590POSTTEXT</h4>

1591<p id="_727" tabindex="-1">

1592The POSTTEXT group is determined after the patients receive chemotherapy. The greatest chemotherapy response, measured as decreases in tumor size and alpha-fetoprotein (AFP) level, occurs after the first two cycles of chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

15936</a>

1594,<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

15957</a>

1596] In addition, a study that evaluated surgical resectability after two versus four cycles of chemotherapy showed that many tumors may be resectable after two cycles.[<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

15976</a>

1598]</p>

1599</section>

1600</section>

1601<section id="_11">

1602<h3 id="_11_toc">

1603Evans Surgical Staging for Childhood Liver Cancer (Historical)</h3>

1604<p id="_12" tabindex="-1">

1605The COG/Evans staging system, based on operative findings and surgical

1606resectability, was used for many years in the United States to group and determine treatment for children with liver

1607cancer (see <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_1869">

1608Table 3</a>

1609).[<a href="#cit/section_3.8">

16108</a>

1611-<a href="#cit/section_3.10">

161210</a>

1613] Currently, other risk stratification systems are used to classify patients and determine treatment strategy. For more information, see <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_695">

1614Table 5</a>

1615.</p>

1616<table id="_1869" class="table-default expandable-container">

1617<caption>

1618Table 3. Definition of Evans Surgical Staging </caption>

1619<colgroup>

1620<col width="50.00%">

1621<col width="50.00%">

1622</colgroup>

1623<THead>

1624<tr>

1625<th>

1626Evans Surgical Stage </th>

1627<th>

1628Definition</th>

1629</tr>

1630</THead>

1631<tbody>

1632<tr>

1633<td>

1634Stage I </td>

1635<td>

1636The tumor is completely resected.</td>

1637</tr>

1638<tr>

1639<td>

1640Stage II </td>

1641<td>

1642Microscopic residual tumor remains after resection.</td>

1643</tr>

1644<tr>

1645<td>

1646Stage III </td>

1647<td>

1648There are no distant metastases and at least one of the following is true: (1) the tumor is either unresectable or the tumor is resected with gross residual tumor; (2) there are positive extrahepatic lymph nodes.</td>

1649</tr>

1650<tr>

1651<td>

1652Stage IV </td>

1653<td>

1654There is distant metastasis, regardless of the extent of liver involvement.</td>

1655</tr>

1657</table>

Pediatr Radiol 48 (4): 536-554, 2018.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=29427028&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=29427028&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1676[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

1677</li>

1678<li id="section_3.5">

1679Aronson DC, Schnater JM, Staalman CR, et al.: Predictive value of the pretreatment extent of disease system in hepatoblastoma: results from the International Society of Pediatric Oncology Liver Tumor Study Group SIOPEL-1 study. J Clin Oncol 23 (6): 1245-52, 2005.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=15718322&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=15718322&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1681</li>

J Pediatr Surg 45 (1): 121-8; discussion 129, 2010.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=20105591&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=20105591&amp;dopt=Abstract">

Br J Surg 102 (1): 108-13, 2015.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=25349947&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=25349947&amp;dopt=Abstract">

1709 <section id="_31">

1710

1711 <h2>

1712Treatment Option Overview for Childhood Liver Cancer</h2>

1713

1714 <div id="_section_31" class="pdq-sections">

1715<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1716<h6>

1717In This Section</h6>

1718<ul>

1719<li>

1720<a href="#_206_toc">

1721Surgery<ul>

1722<li>

1723<a href="#_216_toc">

1724Orthotopic liver transplant</a>

1725</li>

1726<li>

1727<a href="#_734_toc">

1728Surgical resection for metastatic disease</a>

1729</li>

1730</ul>

1731</a>

1732</li>

1733<li>

1734<a href="#_224_toc">

1735Chemotherapy</a>

1736</li>

1737<li>

1738<a href="#_232_toc">

1739Radiation Therapy</a>

1740</li>

1741<li>

1742<a href="#_236_toc">

1743Other Treatment Approaches</a>

1744</li>

1745</ul>

1746</nav>

1747<p id="_32" tabindex="-1">

1748Many of the improvements in survival in childhood cancer have been made using

1749new therapies that have attempted to improve on the best available, accepted

1750therapy. Clinical trials in pediatrics are designed to compare potentially

1751better therapy with therapy that is currently accepted as standard. This

1752comparison may be done in a randomized study of two treatment arms or by

1753evaluating a single new treatment and comparing the results with those previously

1754obtained with standard therapy.

1755</p>

1756<p id="_33" tabindex="-1">

1757Because of the relative rarity of cancer in children, all children with liver

1758cancer should be considered for a clinical trial if available. Treatment

1759planning by a multidisciplinary team of cancer specialists with experience

1760treating tumors of childhood is required to determine and implement optimal

1761treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

17621</a>

1763]</p>

1764<section id="_206">

1765<h3 id="_206_toc">

1766Surgery</h3>

1767<p id="_34" tabindex="-1">

1768Historically, complete surgical resection of the primary tumor has been essential for cure

1769of malignant liver tumors in children.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

17702</a>

1771-<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

17726</a>

1773]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

17747</a>

1775][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1776Level of evidence C1</a>

1777] This approach continues to be the goal of definitive surgical procedures, which are often combined with chemotherapy. The surgeon performs a highly sophisticated liver resection in children and adolescents with primary liver tumors after confirmation of the diagnosis by pathological investigation of intraoperative frozen sections. While complete surgical resection is important for all liver tumors, it is especially important for hepatocellular carcinoma because curative chemotherapy is not available. In patients with advanced hepatoblastoma, postoperative complications are associated with worsened overall survival (OS).[<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

17788</a>

1779]</p>

1780<p id="_207" tabindex="-1">

1781The three surgical options to treat primary pediatric liver cancer include the following:</p>

1782<div class="pdq-content-list">

1783<ul id="_208">

1784<li>

1785Initial surgical resection (alone or with adjuvant chemotherapy).</li>

1786<li>

1787Delayed surgical resection (with neoadjuvant chemotherapy).</li>

1788<li>

1789Orthotopic liver (cadaveric and living donor) transplant (most often with neoadjuvant chemotherapy).</li>

1790</ul>

1791</div>

1792<p id="_212" tabindex="-1">

1793The decision as to which surgical approach to use (e.g., partial hepatectomy, extended resection, or transplant) depends on many factors, including the following:</p>

1794<div class="pdq-content-list">

1795<ul id="_213">

1796<li>

1797PRE-Treatment EXTent of disease (PRETEXT) group and POST-Treatment EXTent of disease (POSTTEXT) group.</li>

1798<li>

1799Size of the primary tumor.</li>

1800<li>

1801Presence of multifocal hepatic disease.</li>

1802<li>

1803Gross vascular involvement.</li>

1804<li>

1805Alpha-fetoprotein (AFP) levels.</li>

1806<li>

1807Whether preoperative chemotherapy is potentially likely to convert an unresectable tumor into a resectable tumor.</li>

1808<li>

1809Whether hepatic disease meets surgical and histopathological criteria for orthotopic liver transplant.</li>

1810</ul>

1811</div>

1812<p id="_209" tabindex="-1">

1813Timing of the surgical approach is critical. Surgeons who have experience performing pediatric liver resections and transplants are involved early in the decision-making process for determining optimal timing and extent of resection. </p>

1814<p id="_2412" tabindex="-1">

1815Early involvement, preferably at diagnosis, with an experienced pediatric liver surgeon is especially important in patients with PRETEXT group III or IV or involvement of major liver vessels (positive annotation factors V [venous] or P [portal]).[<a href="#cit/section_4.9">

18169</a>

1817] Although vascular involvement was initially thought to be a contraindication to resection, experienced liver surgeons are sometimes able to successfully resect the tumor and avoid performing a transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_4.10">

181810</a>

1819-<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

182012</a>

1821]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.13">

182213</a>

1823][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1824Level of evidence C1</a>

1825] Patients with vascular involvement and tumors that have been deemed nonresectable by the pediatric surgical expert should be referred to a transplant center for evaluation to avoid unnecessary delays in evaluation and listing for transplant.</p>

1826<p id="_1873" tabindex="-1">

1827Intraoperative ultrasonography may result in further delineation of tumor extent and location and can affect intraoperative management.[<a href="#cit/section_4.14">

182814</a>

1829] Preoperative infusion of indocyanine green, a fluoroactive agent that is concentrated in the liver and retained by abnormal liver tumors, has also been used to provide visual intraoperative guidance to locate the tumor and assess proximity to surgical margins.[<a href="#cit/section_4.15">

183015</a>

1831]</p>

1832<p id="_210" tabindex="-1">

1833If the tumor is determined to be unresectable, measures to reduce the tumor size to make a complete surgical resection possible need to be considered. These measures include preoperative intravenous chemotherapy, transarterial chemotherapy, or transarterial radioactive therapy. These efforts must be carefully coordinated with the surgical team to facilitate planning of resection. Prolonged chemotherapy can lead to unnecessary delays and, in rare cases, tumor progression. If the tumor can be completely excised by an experienced surgical team, less postoperative chemotherapy may be needed. Incomplete resection must be avoided because patients who undergo rescue transplants of incompletely resected tumors have an inferior outcome, compared with patients who undergo transplant as the primary surgical therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

183416</a>

1835][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1836Level of evidence C1</a>

1837] Accomplishing the appropriate surgery at resection is critical. </p>

1838<p id="_214" tabindex="-1">

1839The approach taken by the Children's Oncology Group (COG) in North American clinical trials is to perform surgery initially when a complete resection can be done with a simple, negative-margin hemihepatectomy. The COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00980460">

1840AHEP0731 (NCT00980460)</a>

1841 trial studied the use of PRETEXT and POSTTEXT to determine the optimal approach and timing of surgery. POSTTEXT imaging grouping was performed after two and four cycles of chemotherapy to determine the optimal time for definitive surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

18426</a>

1843,<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

184417</a>

1845] For more information, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_10">

1846Tumor Stratification by Imaging and Evans Surgical Staging for Childhood Liver Cancer</a>

1847 section.</p>

1848<section id="_216">

1849<h4 id="_216_toc">

1850Orthotopic liver transplant</h4>

1851<p id="_384" tabindex="-1">

1852Liver transplants have been associated with significant success in the treatment of children with unresectable hepatic tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_4.18">

185318</a>

1854]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.19">

185519</a>

1856-<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

185721</a>

1858][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1859Level of evidence C1</a>

1860] A review of the world experience has documented a posttransplant survival rate of 70% to 80% for children with hepatoblastomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

186116</a>

1862,<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

186322</a>

1864-<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

186524</a>

1866] Intravenous vascular invasion, positive lymph nodes, and contiguous extrahepatic spread did not have a significant adverse effect on outcome. Adjuvant chemotherapy after transplant may decrease the risk of tumor recurrence, but its use has not been studied definitively in a randomized clinical trial.[<a href="#cit/section_4.25">

186725</a>

1868]</p>

1869<p id="_385" tabindex="-1">

1870Evidence (orthotopic liver transplant):</p>

1871<div class="pdq-content-list">

1872<ol id="_623">

1873<li>

1874The United Network for Organ Sharing (UNOS) database was queried for all patients younger than 18 years with a primary malignant liver tumor who underwent an orthotopic liver transplant between 1987 and 2012 (N = 544). The patients were diagnosed with hepatoblastoma (n = 376, 70%), hepatocellular carcinoma (n = 84, 15%), and other tumors (n = 84, 15%). Patients with hepatocellular carcinoma were older, more often hospitalized at the time of transplant, and more likely to receive a cadaveric organ than were patients with hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

187526</a>

1876]<div class="pdq-content-list">

1877<ol id="_1908" class="lower-alpha">

1878<li>

1879The 5-year patient survival rate was 73%, and the graft survival rate was 74% for the entire cohort, with most deaths resulting from malignancy. On multivariate analysis, independent predictors of 5-year patient and graft survival included the following:<div class="pdq-content-list">

1880<ol id="_1909" class="lower-roman">

1881<li>

1882 Diagnosis.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1883<ul id="_1895">

1884<li>

1885For the study period of 1987 to 2012, the 5-year survival rate was 76% and the graft survival rate was 77% for patients with hepatoblastoma; the survival and graft survival rates were 63% for patients with hepatocellular carcinoma.</li>

1886<li>

1887For the study period of 2009 to 2012, the 3-year survival and graft survival rates were 84% for patients with hepatoblastoma; the survival and graft survival rates were 85% for patients with hepatocellular carcinoma.</li>

1888</ul>

1889</div>

1890</li>

1891<li>

1892Transplant era.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1893<ul id="_1896">

1894<li>

1895The death rate by hazard ratio was 1.0 for the period before 2002, 0.72 for the period of 2002 to 2009, and 0.54 for the period of 2009 to 2012.</li>

1896</ul>

1897</div>

1898</li>

1899<li>

1900Medical condition at transplant.<div class="pdq-content-list">

1901<ul id="_1897">

1902<li>

1903For hepatoblastoma patients, the survival rate by hazard ratio was 1.0 for hospitalized patients versus 1.81 for nonhospitalized patients at the time of transplant. </li>

1904<li>

1905For hepatocellular carcinoma patients, the survival rate by hazard ratio was 1.0 for hospitalized patients versus 1.92 for nonhospitalized patients.</li>

1906<li>

1907Patients hospitalized in the intensive care unit did not fare worse than patients not in the intensive care unit.</li>

1908</ul>

1909</div>

1910</li>

1911</ol>

1912</div>

1913</li>

1914</ol>

1915</div>

1916</li>

1917<li>

1918A report of 149 patients with hepatocellular carcinoma younger than 21 years who underwent transplants between 1987 and 2015 used detailed data collected at all U.S. pediatric transplant centers by the U.S. Scientific Registry of Transplant Recipients.[<a href="#cit/section_4.18">

191918</a>

1920] <div class="pdq-content-list">

1921<ul id="_1903">

1922<li>

1923The 1-year graft survival rate was about 85%, which did not differ from the survival for patients with hepatoblastoma or biliary atresia. Survival rates continued to decline over time, from 85% at 1 year to 52% at 5 years and 43% at 10 years, a more dramatic decline than what was seen for hepatoblastoma or biliary atresia.</li>

1924<li>

1925The survival after transplant did not differ from that of adults who underwent transplant for hepatocellular carcinoma.</li>

1926<li>

1927Of the patients with hepatocellular carcinoma, 22 had hepatocellular carcinoma diagnosed after transplant for medical cirrhotic disease such as tyrosinemia. They had a superior outcome, but it was not statistically significant compared with the rest of the 149 patients.</li>

1928</ul>

1929</div>

1930</li>

1931<li>

1932A review of the Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program database and many single-institution series have reported results similar to the UNOS database study described above.[<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

193311</a>

1934,<a href="#cit/section_4.19">

193519</a>

1936-<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

193721</a>

1938,<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

193927</a>

1940]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

194124</a>

1942][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1943Level of evidence C1</a>

1944]</li>

1945<li>

1946In a three-institution study of children with hepatocellular carcinoma, the overall 5-year disease-free survival rate was approximately 60%.[<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

194728</a>

1948]</li>

1949<li>

1950In a study that used the Society of Pediatric Liver Transplantation (SPLIT) database to identify patients who underwent liver transplant between 2011 and 2019, the following was reported:[<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

195129</a>

1952][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1953Level of evidence C2</a>

1954]<div class="pdq-content-list">

1955<ul id="_2433">

1956<li>

1957 The 3-year EFS rate was 81% for patients with hepatoblastoma who received a transplant (n = 157). </li>

1958<li>

1959The 3-year EFS rate was 62% for patients with hepatocellular carcinoma who received a transplant (n = 18). </li>

1960<li>

1961Of the patients who received a transplant to treat hepatoblastoma, 6.9% had PRETEXT II disease and 15.3% had POSTTEXT I/II disease. </li>

1962<li>

1963Tumor extent did not impact survival (<em>

1964P</em>

1965 = NS). </li>

1966<li>

1967Patients who received transplants for salvage (n = 13) and patients who received transplants for primary hepatoblastoma had similar 3-year EFS rates (62% vs. 78%; <em>

1968P</em>

1969 = NS). </li>

1970<li>

1971Among patients who received transplants for hepatocellular carcinoma, the 3-year EFS rate was poorer in older patients (38% for patients aged &#8805;8 years vs. 86% for patients aged &lt;8 years; <em>

1972P</em>

1973 &lt; .001).</li>

1974</ul>

1975</div>

1976</li>

1977</ol>

1978</div>

1979<p id="_625" tabindex="-1">

1980Application of the Milan criteria for UNOS selection of recipients of deceased donor livers is controversial.[<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

198130</a>

1982,<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

198331</a>

1984] The Milan criteria for liver transplant are directed toward adults with cirrhosis and hepatocellular carcinoma. The criteria do not apply to children and adolescents with hepatocellular carcinoma, especially those without cirrhosis. </p>

1985<p id="_1982" tabindex="-1">

1986Cirrhosis is an underlying risk factor for the development of hepatocellular carcinoma in children who suffer from certain diseases or conditions. These diseases include perinatally acquired hepatitis B, hepatorenal tyrosinemia, progressive familial intrahepatic cholestasis, glycogen storage disease, Alagille syndrome, and other conditions. Improvements in screening methodology have allowed for earlier identification and treatment of some of these conditions, as well as monitoring for development of hepatocellular carcinoma. Nevertheless, because of the poor prognosis of patients with hepatocellular carcinoma, liver transplant should be considered for diseases or conditions that have resulted in early findings of cirrhosis, before the development of liver failure or malignancy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

198732</a>

1988]</p>

1989<p id="_1983" tabindex="-1">

1990Living-donor liver transplant for hepatic malignancy is more common in children and the outcome is similar to those undergoing cadaveric liver transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

199133</a>

1992,<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

199334</a>

1994] In patients with hepatocellular carcinoma, gross vascular invasion, distant metastases, lymph node involvement, tumor size, and male sex were significant risk factors for recurrence. In one report, 33 patients with hepatoblastoma and 10 patients with hepatocellular carcinoma were treated with living-donor liver transplants. For the hepatoblastoma patients, the 5-year OS rate was 87.4%, and the event-free survival (EFS) rate was 75.8%. The 5-year OS and EFS rates were 75.4% for the patients with hepatocellular carcinoma. The presence of renal vein invasion was associated with an increased incidence of recurrence and death (<em>

1995P</em>

1996 = .28).[<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

199735</a>

1998][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1999Level of evidence C1</a>

2000]</p>

2001</section>

2002<section id="_734">

2003<h4 id="_734_toc">

2004Surgical resection for metastatic disease</h4>

2005<p id="_735" tabindex="-1">

2006Surgical resection of metastatic disease is often recommended, but the rate of cure in children with hepatoblastoma has not been fully determined. Resection of metastases may be done for areas of locally invasive disease (e.g., diaphragm) and isolated brain metastases. Resection of pulmonary metastases should be considered if the number of metastases is limited.[<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

200736</a>

2008-<a href="#cit/section_4.39">

200939</a>

2010] In an American study of 38 patients who presented at diagnosis with pulmonary metastases, only nine patients underwent surgical resection. The timing of pulmonary resection in relation to definitive resection of the primary tumor varied (two patients before, five patients simultaneously, and two patients after primary resection). Eight of the nine patients survived. Of 20 children with relapse restricted to the lungs, all patients received salvage chemotherapy, 8 patients had a thoracotomy and pulmonary metastasectomy, and 5 patients had a thoracotomy and biopsy. Among the 13 patients who had surgery, only 4 were long-term survivors, 2 of whom presented with stage I disease and 2 of whom presented with stage IV disease.[<a href="#cit/section_4.38">

201138</a>

2012]</p>

2013<p id="_1990" tabindex="-1">

2014 Radiofrequency ablation has also been used to treat oligometastatic hepatoblastoma when patients prefer to avoid surgical metastasectomy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.40">

201540</a>

2016][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2017Level of evidence C1</a>

2018]</p>

2019</section>

2020</section>

2021<section id="_224">

2022<h3 id="_224_toc">

2023Chemotherapy</h3>

2024<p id="_736" tabindex="-1">

2025Chemotherapy regimens used in the treatment of hepatoblastoma and hepatocellular carcinoma are described in their respective sections. Chemotherapy has been much more successful in the treatment of hepatoblastoma than in hepatocellular carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

20266</a>

2027,<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

202827</a>

2029,<a href="#cit/section_4.41">

203041</a>

2031] For more information, see the sections on <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_373">

2032Treatment of Hepatoblastoma</a>

2033 and <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_374">

2034Treatment of Hepatocellular Carcinoma</a>

2035.</p>

2036<p id="_737" tabindex="-1">

2037The standard of care in the United States is preoperative chemotherapy when the tumor is unresectable and postoperative chemotherapy after complete resection, even if preoperative chemotherapy has already been given.[<a href="#cit/section_4.42">

203842</a>

2039] Treatment with preoperative chemotherapy has been shown to benefit children with hepatoblastoma; however, the use of postoperative chemotherapy after definitive surgical resection or liver transplant has not been investigated in a randomized fashion. </p>

2040</section>

2041<section id="_232">

2042<h3 id="_232_toc">

2043Radiation Therapy</h3>

2044<p id="_234" tabindex="-1">

2045Radiation therapy, even in combination with chemotherapy, has not cured children with unresectable hepatic tumors. A study of 154 patients with hepatoblastoma showed that radiation therapy and/or second resection of positive margins may not be necessary in some patients with incompletely resected hepatoblastoma and microscopic residual tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_4.43">

204643</a>

2047] Although there is no standard indication, radiation therapy may have a role in the management of patients with incompletely resected hepatoblastomas.[<a href="#cit/section_4.44">

204844</a>

2049] Stereotactic body radiation therapy is a safe and effective alternative treatment that has been successfully used in adult patients with hepatocellular carcinoma who are unable to undergo liver ablation/resection.[<a href="#cit/section_4.45">

205045</a>

2051] This highly conformal radiotherapeutic technique, when available, may be considered on an individual basis in children with hepatocellular carcinoma.</p>

2052</section>

2053<section id="_236">

2054<h3 id="_236_toc">

2055Other Treatment Approaches</h3>

2056<p id="_237" tabindex="-1">

2057Other treatment approaches include the following:</p>

2058<div class="pdq-content-list">

2059<ul id="_1974">

2060<li>

2061<strong>

2062Transarterial chemoembolization (TACE):</strong>

2063 TACE is an image-guided, minimally invasive, nonsurgical procedure that is used to treat malignant lesions in the liver. The procedure uses a catheter to deliver both chemotherapy medication and embolization materials into the blood vessels that lead to the tumor. The arterial catheter route is image guided, most often via the hepatic artery, and perfusion of the tumor by the targeted artery may be confirmed by imaging before therapeutic injection. This procedure allows for the treatment of tumors that are not accessible with conventional surgery or radiation treatments. TACE has been used for patients with inoperable hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.46">

206446</a>

2065-<a href="#cit/section_4.48">

206648</a>

2067] This procedure has also been used in a few children to successfully shrink tumors to permit resections.[<a href="#cit/section_4.47">

206847</a>

2069] </li>

2070<li>

2071<strong>

2072Transarterial radioembolization (TARE):</strong>

2073 TARE is an image-guided, minimally invasive, nonsurgical procedure that delivers radiation therapy to treat tumors in the liver. This procedure delivers radioactive beads and blocks arterial flow within the tumor to keep the radiation inside the tumor. Glass or resin microspheres, coated most commonly with yttrium Y 90 (90Y), are delivered to the tumor via catheters placed in arteries that supply the tumor. Usually, the hepatic artery or its branches are used, but tumors may be partially supplied by parasitized surrounding vessels. Because of the risk of radiation delivery to the nearby lung, technetium Tc 99m microaggregated albumin imaging is performed with delivery via the catheter that is in place before the administration of radioactive beads to carefully measure radiation exposure to the lung. If calculations determine that lung exposure is unsafe, TARE is not pursued. TARE with 90Y has been used in children with hepatoblastoma (n = 2) and hepatocellular carcinoma (n = 2) who have unresectable tumors. After treatment with 90Y TARE, all tumors were completely resected.[<a href="#cit/section_4.49">

207449</a>

2075][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2076Level of evidence C3</a>

2077]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.50">

207850</a>

2079][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2080Level of evidence C2</a>

2081] This approach has also been used for palliation in children with hepatocellular carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.51">

208251</a>

2083] For more information, see <a href="/types/liver/hp/adult-liver-treatment-pdq">

2084Primary Liver Cancer Treatment</a>

2085.</li>

2086<li>

2087<strong>

2088High-intensity focused ultrasonography (HIFU):</strong>

2089 HIFU is a noninvasive treatment for a wide range of tumors and diseases. HIFU uses an ultrasound transducer, similar to the ones used for diagnostic imaging, but with much higher energy. The transducer focuses sound waves to generate heat at a single point within the body and destroy the target tissue. The tissue can get as hot as 66&#176;C in only 20 seconds. This process is repeated as many times as necessary until the target tissue is destroyed. Magnetic resonance imaging is used to plan the treatment and monitor the amount of heat in real time. A combination of chemotherapy followed by TACE and HIFU showed promising results in China for children with PRETEXT III and PRETEXT IV malignant liver tumors, some of whom had resectable tumors but did not undergo surgery because of parent refusal.[<a href="#cit/section_4.52">

209052</a>

2091]</li>

2092</ul>

2093</div>

2094</section>

2313 <section id="_940">

2314

2315 <h2>

2316Special Considerations for the Treatment of Children With Cancer</h2>

2317

2318 <div id="_section_940" class="pdq-sections">

2319<p id="_941" tabindex="-1">

2320Cancer in children and adolescents is rare, although the overall incidence has been slowly increasing since 1975.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

23211</a>

2322] Children and adolescents with

2323cancer should be referred to medical centers that have multidisciplinary teams

2324of cancer specialists with experience treating pediatric cancers. This multidisciplinary team approach incorporates the skills

2325of the following health care professionals and others to ensure that children receive treatment, supportive care, and rehabilitation

2326that will achieve optimal survival and quality of life:</p>

2327<div class="pdq-content-list">

2328<ul id="_942">

2329<li>

2330Primary care physicians.</li>

2331<li>

2332Pediatric surgeons and transplant surgeons.</li>

2333<li>

2334Radiation oncologists. </li>

2335<li>

2336Pediatric medical oncologists/hematologists.</li>

2337<li>

2338Rehabilitation specialists.</li>

2339<li>

2340Pediatric nurse specialists.</li>

2341<li>

2342Social workers.</li>

2343<li>

2344Child life professionals.</li>

2345<li>

2346Psychologists.</li>

2347<li>

2348Nutritionists.</li>

2349</ul>

2350</div>

2351<p id="_943" tabindex="-1">

2352For specific information about supportive care for children and adolescents with cancer, see the summaries on <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care">

2353Supportive and Palliative Care</a>

2354.</p>

2355<p id="_944" tabindex="-1">

2356The American Academy of Pediatrics has outlined guidelines for the role of pediatric cancer

2357centers in the treatment of children and adolescents with cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

23582</a>

2359] At these centers, clinical trials are available for most types of cancer

2360that occur in children and adolescents, and the opportunity to participate is offered to most patients and their families. Clinical trials for

2361children and adolescents with cancer are generally designed to compare

2362potentially better therapy with current standard therapy.

2363Most of the progress made in identifying curative therapies for

2364childhood cancers has been achieved through clinical trials. Information about

2365ongoing clinical trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

2366NCI website</a>

2367.</p>

2368<p id="_945" tabindex="-1">

2369Dramatic improvements in survival have been achieved for children and adolescents with cancer. Between 1975 and 2020, childhood cancer mortality decreased by more than 50%.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

23701</a>

2371,<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

23723</a>

2373,<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

23744</a>

2375] Childhood and adolescent cancer survivors require close monitoring because side effects of cancer therapy may persist or develop months or years after treatment. For specific information about the incidence, type, and monitoring of late effects in childhood and adolescent cancer survivors, see <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq">

2376Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer</a>

2377.</p>

2378<h6 do-not-show="toc">

2379References</h6>

2532<section id="_568">

2533<h3 id="_568_toc">

2534Incidence</h3>

2535<p id="_353" tabindex="-1">

2536The annual incidence of hepatoblastoma in the United States has increased (more than doubled), from 0.8 (1975&#8211;1983) to 2.0 (2018) cases per 1 million children aged 19 years and younger.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

25371</a>

2538-<a href="#cit/section_6.3">

25393</a>

2540] The cause for this increase is unknown, but the improved survival of premature infants with very low birth weight, which is known to be associated with hepatoblastoma, may contribute.[<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

25414</a>

2542] In Japan, the risk of hepatoblastoma in children who weighed less than 1,000 g at birth is 15 times the risk in children with normal birth weight.[<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

25435</a>

2544] Other data have confirmed the high incidence of hepatoblastoma in premature infants with very low birth weight.[<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

25456</a>

2546] Attempts to identify factors resulting from treatment of infants born prematurely have not revealed any suggestive causation of the increased incidence of hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.4">

25474</a>

2548]</p>

2549<p id="_380" tabindex="-1">

2550The

2551age of onset of liver cancer in children is related to tumor histology. Hepatoblastomas usually occur before the age of 3 years, and approximately 90% of malignant liver tumors in children aged 4 years and younger are hepatoblastomas.[<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

25527</a>

2553] </p>

2554</section>
