2654<section id="_574">

2655<h4 id="_574_toc">

2656Aicardi syndrome</h4>

2657<p id="_575" tabindex="-1">

2658Aicardi syndrome is presumed to be an X-linked condition reported exclusively in females, leading to the hypothesis that a mutated gene on the X chromosome causes lethality in males. The syndrome is classically defined as agenesis of the corpus callosum, chorioretinal lacunae, and infantile spasms, with a characteristic facies. Additional brain, eye, and costovertebral defects are often found.[<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

26598</a>

2660] </p>

2661</section>

2662<section id="_509">

2663<h4 id="_509_toc">

2664Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome and hemihyperplasia</h4>

2665<p id="_510" tabindex="-1">

2666The incidence of hepatoblastoma increases 1,000-fold to 10,000-fold in infants and children with Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

266710</a>

2668,<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

266919</a>

2670] The risk of hepatoblastoma also increases in patients with hemihyperplasia, previously termed hemihypertrophy, a condition that results in asymmetry between the right and left side of the body when a body part grows faster than normal.[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

267120</a>

2672,<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

267321</a>

2674]</p>

2675<p id="_511" tabindex="-1">

2676Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome is most commonly caused by epigenetic changes and is sporadic. The syndrome may also be caused by genetic mutations and be familial. Either mechanism can be associated with an increased incidence of embryonal tumors, including Wilms tumor and hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

267710</a>

2678] The expression of both <em class="gene-name">

2679IGFR2</em>

2680 alleles and ensuing increased expression of insulin-like growth factor 2 (IGF-2) has been implicated in the macrosomia and embryonal tumors seen in patients with Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome.[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

268110</a>

2682,<a href="#cit/section_6.22">

268322</a>

2684] The types of embryonal tumors associated with sporadic Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome have frequently undergone somatic changes in the Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome locus and IGF-2.[<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

268523</a>

2686,<a href="#cit/section_6.24">

268724</a>

2688] The genetics of tumors in children with hemihyperplasia have not been clearly defined.</p>

2689<p id="_512" tabindex="-1">

2690To detect abdominal malignancies at an early stage, all children with Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome or isolated hemihyperplasia undergo regular screening for multiple tumor types by abdominal ultrasonography.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

269121</a>

2692] Screening using alpha-fetoprotein (AFP) levels has also been quite helpful in the early detection of hepatoblastoma in these children.[<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

269325</a>

2694] Because the hepatoblastomas that are discovered early are small, treatment may minimize the use of adjuvant therapy after surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

269519</a>

2696] However, a careful compilation of published data on 1,370 children with (epi)genotyped Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome demonstrated that the prevalence of hepatoblastoma was 4.7% in those with Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome caused by chromosome 11p15 paternal uniparental disomy, less than 1% in the two types of alteration in imprinting control regions, and absent in <em>

2697CDKN1C</em>

2698 mutation.[<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

269926</a>

2700] The authors recommended that only children with Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome caused by uniparental disomy be screened for hepatoblastoma using abdominal ultrasonography and AFP levels every 3 months from age 3 months to 5 years.</p>

2701</section>

2702<section id="_513">

2703<h4 id="_513_toc">

2704Familial adenomatous polyposis</h4>

2705<p id="_514" tabindex="-1">

2706There is an association between

2707hepatoblastoma and familial adenomatous polyposis (FAP). Children in families

2708that carry the <em class="gene-name">

2709APC</em>

2710 gene have an 800-fold increased risk of hepatoblastoma. Screening for hepatoblastoma in members of families with FAP using ultrasonography and AFP levels is controversial because hepatoblastoma has been reported to occur in less than 1% of FAP family members.[<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

271111</a>

2712-<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

271313</a>

2714,<a href="#cit/section_6.27">

271527</a>

2716] However, one study of 50 consecutive children with apparent sporadic hepatoblastoma reported that five children (10%) had <em class="gene-name">

2717APC</em>

2718 germline mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_6.27">

271927</a>

2720]</p>

2721<p id="_1876" tabindex="-1">

2722Current evidence cannot rule out the possibility that predisposition to hepatoblastoma may be limited to a specific subset of <em class="gene-name">

2723APC</em>

2724 mutations. Another study of children with hepatoblastoma found a predominance of the mutation in the 5' region of the gene, but some patients had mutations closer to the 3' region.[<a href="#cit/section_6.28">

272528</a>

2726] This preliminary study provides some evidence that screening children with hepatoblastoma for <em class="gene-name">

2727APC</em>

2728 mutations and colon cancer may be appropriate.</p>

2729<p id="_526" tabindex="-1">

2730In the absence of <em class="gene-name">

2731APC</em>

2732 germline mutations, childhood

2733hepatoblastomas do not have somatic mutations in the <em class="gene-name">

2734APC</em>

2735 gene; however, hepatoblastomas frequently have mutations in the <em class="gene-name">

2736CTNNB1</em>

2737 gene, the

2738function of which is closely related to <em class="gene-name">

2739APC</em>

2740.[<a href="#cit/section_6.29">

274129</a>

2742]</p>

2743</section>

2744<section id="_1904">

2745<h4 id="_1904_toc">

2746Screening children predisposed to hepatoblastoma</h4>

2747<p id="_1905" tabindex="-1">

2748An American Association for Cancer Research publication suggested that all children with more-than-a-1% risk of developing hepatoblastoma undergo screening. This includes patients with Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome, hemihyperplasia, Simpson-Golabi-Behmel syndrome, and trisomy 18 syndrome. Screening is by abdominal ultrasonography and AFP determination every 3 months from birth (or diagnosis) through the fourth birthday, which will identify 90% to 95% of hepatoblastomas that develop in these children.[<a href="#cit/section_6.30">

274930</a>

2750]</p>

2751</section>

2752</section>

2753<section id="_1934">

2754<h3 id="_1934_toc">

2755Genomics of Hepatoblastoma</h3>

2756<section id="_sm_CDR0000779397_6">

2757<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779397_6_toc">

2758Molecular features of hepatoblastoma</h4>

2759<p id="_sm_CDR0000779397_730" tabindex="-1">

2760Genomic findings related to hepatoblastoma include the following:</p>

2761<div class="pdq-content-list">

2762<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779397_731">

2763<li>

2764The frequency of mutations in hepatoblastoma, as determined by three groups using whole-exome sequencing, was very low (approximately three variants per tumor) in children younger than 5 years.[<a href="#cit/section_6.31">

276531</a>

2766-<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

276734</a>

2768] A pediatric pan-cancer genomics study found that hepatoblastoma had the lowest gene mutation rate among all childhood cancers studied.[<a href="#cit/section_6.35">

276935</a>

2770]</li>

2771<li>

2772Hepatoblastoma is primarily a disease of WNT pathway activation. The primary mechanism for WNT pathway activation is <em class="gene-name">

2773CTNNB1</em>

2774 activating mutations/deletions involving exon 3. <em class="gene-name">

2775CTNNB1</em>

2776 mutations have been reported in more than 80% of cases.[<a href="#cit/section_6.31">

277731</a>

2778,<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

277933</a>

2780,<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

278134</a>

2782,<a href="#cit/section_6.36">

278336</a>

2784,<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

278537</a>

2786] A less common cause of WNT pathway activation in hepatoblastoma is mutations in <em class="gene-name">

2787APC</em>

2788 associated with familial adenomatosis polyposis coli.[<a href="#cit/section_6.36">

278936</a>

2790]</li>

2791<li>

2792<em class="gene-name">

2793NFE2L2</em>

2794 mutations were identified in 10 of 174 (6%), 4 of 88 (5%), and 5 of 112 (4%) cases of hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

279533</a>

2796,<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

279734</a>

2798,<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

279937</a>

2800] The presence of <em class="gene-name">

2801NFE2L2</em>

2802 mutations was associated with a lower survival rate.[<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

280337</a>

2804] </li>

2805<li>

2806Similar <em class="gene-name">

2807NFE2L2</em>

2808 mutations have been found in many types of cancer, including hepatocellular carcinoma. These mutations render NFE2L2 insensitive to KEAP1-mediated degradation, leading to activation of the NFE2L2-KEAP1 pathway, which activates resistance to oxidative stress and is believed to confer resistance to chemotherapy.</li>

2809<li>

2810<em class="gene-name">

2811TERT</em>

2812 and <em class="gene-name">

2813TP53</em>

2814 mutations, which are common in adults with hepatocellular carcinoma,[<a href="#cit/section_6.38">

281538</a>

2816] are uncommon in pediatric hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.31">

281731</a>

2818,<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

281933</a>

2820,<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

282134</a>

2822,<a href="#cit/section_6.36">

282336</a>

2824] Hepatoblastoma cases with <em class="gene-name">

2825TERT</em>

2826 mutations occur at a significantly older age, compared with hepatoblastoma cases without <em class="gene-name">

2827TERT</em>

2828 mutations (median age at diagnosis, approximately 10 years vs. 1.4 years).[<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

282937</a>

2830]</li>

2831<li>

2832Uniparental disomy at 11p15.5 with loss of the maternal allele was reported in 6 of 15 cases of hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.39">

283339</a>

2834] This finding has been confirmed in genomic characterization studies, in which 30% to 40% of cases showed allelic imbalance at the 11p15 locus.[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

283534</a>

2836,<a href="#cit/section_6.36">

283736</a>

2838,<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

283937</a>

2840]</li>

2841</ul>

2842</div>

2843<p id="_sm_CDR0000779397_754" tabindex="-1">

2844Gene expression and epigenetic profiling have been used to identify biological subtypes of hepatoblastoma and to evaluate the prognostic significance of these subtypes.[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

284533</a>

2846,<a href="#cit/section_6.36">

284736</a>

2848,<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

284937</a>

2850,<a href="#cit/section_6.40">

285140</a>

2852]</p>

2853<div class="pdq-content-list">

2854<ul id="_sm_CDR0000779397_755">

2855<li>

2856A 16-gene expression signature divided hepatoblastoma cases into two subsets,[<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

285737</a>

2858,<a href="#cit/section_6.40">

285940</a>

2860] C1 and C2. The C1 subtype included most of the well-differentiated fetal (<em>

2861pure fetal</em>

2862) histology cases. The C2 subtype showed a more immature pattern and was associated with higher rates of metastatic disease at diagnosis. In a study of 174 hepatoblastoma patients, the C2 subtype was a significant predictor of poor outcome in multivariable analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.37">

286337</a>

2864]</li>

2865<li>

2866A second research group also found that gene expression profiling could be used to identify subsets of hepatoblastoma with favorable versus unfavorable prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.33">

286733</a>

2868] The unfavorable prognosis group showed elevated expression of genes associated with embryonic stem cell and progenitor cells (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

2869LIN28B</em>

2870, <em class="gene-name">

2871SALL4</em>

2872, and <em class="gene-name">

2873HMGA2</em>

2874). The favorable prognosis group showed elevated expression of genes associated with liver differentiation (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

2875HNF1A</em>

2876).</li>

2877<li>

2878A gene expression signature at chromosome 14q32 (e.g., <em class="gene-name">

2879DLK1</em>

2880) was identified, with a stronger expression signal being associated with higher risk of treatment failure.[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

288134</a>

2882] A strong 14q32 expression signature was also observed in fetal liver tissue, further supporting the concept that hepatoblastoma cases with biological characteristics similar to those of hepatic precursor cells have an inferior prognosis.</li>

2883<li>

2884Epigenetic profiling of hepatoblastoma has been used to identify molecularly defined hepatoblastoma subtypes. Tumors from 113 patients with hepatoblastoma were evaluated using DNA methylation arrays. Two distinctive subtypes were identified, epigenetic cluster A and B (Epi-CA and Epi-CB).[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

288534</a>

2886] The methylation profile of Epi-CB resembled that of early embryonal/fetal phases of liver development. The methylation profile of Epi-CA was similar to that of late fetal or postnatal liver phases. Event-free survival was significantly lower for patients with the Epi-CB subtype than for those with the Epi-CA subtype.[<a href="#cit/section_6.34">

288734</a>

2888]</li>

2889</ul>

2890</div>

2891<p id="_sm_CDR0000779397_756" tabindex="-1">

2892Delineating the clinical applications of the genomic, transcriptomic, and epigenomic profiling methods described above for the risk classification of patients with hepatoblastoma will require independent validation, which is one of the objectives of the ongoing Paediatric Hepatic International Tumour Trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03017326">

2893PHITT [NCT03017326]</a>

2894).</p>

2895</section>

2896</section>

2897<section id="_366">

2898<h3 id="_366_toc">

2899Diagnosis</h3>

2900<section id="_1877">

2901<h4 id="_1877_toc">

2902Biopsy</h4>

2903<p id="_367" tabindex="-1">

2904A biopsy is always indicated to secure the diagnosis of a pediatric liver tumor, except for the following circumstances:</p>

2905<div class="pdq-content-list">

2906<ul id="_368">

2907<li>

2908Infantile hepatic hemangioma. Biopsy is not indicated for patients with infantile hemangioma of the liver with classic findings on magnetic resonance imaging (MRI). If the diagnosis is in doubt after high-quality imaging, a confirmatory biopsy is done.</li>

2909<li>

2910Focal nodular hyperplasia. Biopsy may not be indicated or may be delayed for patients with focal nodular hyperplasia with classic features on MRI using hepatocyte-specific contrast agent. If the diagnosis is in doubt, a confirmatory biopsy is done.</li>

2911<li>

2912Children's Oncology Group (COG) surgical guidelines (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00980460">

2913AHEP0731 [NCT00980460]</a>

2914 appendix) recommend tumor resection at diagnosis without preoperative chemotherapy in children with PRE-Treatment EXTent of disease (PRETEXT) group I tumors and PRETEXT group II tumors with greater than 1 cm radiographic margin on the vena cava and middle hepatic and portal veins. Therefore, biopsy is not usually recommended in this circumstance.</li>

2915<li>

2916Infantile hepatic choriocarcinoma. In patients with infantile hepatic choriocarcinoma, which can be diagnosed by imaging and markedly elevated beta-human chorionic gonadotropin (beta-hCG), chemotherapy without biopsy is often indicated.[<a href="#cit/section_6.41">

291741</a>

2918]</li>

2919</ul>

2920</div>

2921</section>

2922<section id="_1878">

2923<h4 id="_1878_toc">

2924Tumor markers</h4>

2925<p id="_369" tabindex="-1">

2926The AFP and beta-hCG tumor markers are helpful in the diagnosis and management of liver tumors. Although AFP is elevated in most children with hepatic malignancy, it is not pathognomonic for a malignant liver tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_6.42">

292742</a>

2928] The AFP level can be elevated with either a benign tumor or a malignant solid tumor. Markedly elevated AFP not caused by the tumor is normal in neonates and steadily falls after birth. The half-life of AFP is 5 to 7 days, and by age 1 year, it should be in the normal range, less than 10 ng/mL.[<a href="#cit/section_6.43">

292943</a>

2930,<a href="#cit/section_6.44">

293144</a>

2932] Beta-hCG levels may also be elevated in children with hepatoblastoma or hepatocellular carcinoma, which may result in isosexual precocity in boys.[<a href="#cit/section_6.45">

293345</a>

2934,<a href="#cit/section_6.46">

293546</a>

2936]</p>

2937</section>

2938</section>

2939<section id="_581">

2940<h3 id="_581_toc">

2941Prognosis and Prognostic Factors</h3>

2942<section id="_2391">

2943<h4 id="_2391_toc">

2944Prognosis</h4>

2945<p id="_569" tabindex="-1">

2946The 5-year overall survival (OS) rate for children with

2947hepatoblastoma is 70%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.47">

294847</a>

2949,<a href="#cit/section_6.48">

295048</a>

2951] Neonates with hepatoblastoma have outcomes comparable to older children up to age 5 years.[<a href="#cit/section_6.49">

295249</a>

2953] </p>

2954<p id="_1836" tabindex="-1">

2955A collaborative group consisting of four study groups (International Childhood Liver Tumors Strategy Group [SIOPEL], COG, Gesellschaft f&#252;r P&#228;diatrische Onkologie und H&#228;matologie, and Japanese Study Group for Pediatric Liver Tumor [JPLT]) was named the Children's Hepatic Tumor International Collaboration (CHIC). The CHIC group analyzed survival in a collaborative database of 1,605 patients with hepatoblastoma treated in eight separate multicenter clinical trials, with central review of all tumor imaging and histological details.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

295650</a>

2957] Patients who underwent orthotopic liver transplant are included in all of the international study results.[<a href="#cit/section_6.51">

295851</a>

2959] </p>

2960<p id="_1850" tabindex="-1">

2961Survival rates at 5 years, unrelated to annotation factors, were found to be the following:</p>

2962<div class="pdq-content-list">

2963<ul id="_1837">

2964<li>

296590% for patients with PRETEXT I group tumors.</li>

2966<li>

296783% for patients with PRETEXT II group tumors.</li>

2968<li>

296973% for patients with PRETEXT III group tumors.</li>

2970<li>

297152% for patients with PRETEXT IV group tumors.</li>

2972</ul>

2973</div>

2974<p id="_1851" tabindex="-1">

2975When each annotation factor was examined separately, regardless of the PRETEXT group or other annotation factors present in each patient, the 5-year OS rates were found to be the following:</p>

2976<div class="pdq-content-list">

2977<ul id="_1852">

2978<li>

297951% for patients with positive V (involvement of all three hepatic veins and/or inferior vena cava).</li>

2980<li>

298149% for patients with positive P (involvement of both right and left portal veins).</li>

2982<li>

298353% for patients with positive E (contiguous extrahepatic tumor).</li>

2984<li>

298552% for patients with positive F (multifocal).</li>

2986<li>

298751% for patients with positive R (tumor rupture).</li>

2988<li>

298941% for patients with positive M (distant metastasis).</li>

2990</ul>

2991</div>

2992<p id="_2424" tabindex="-1">

2993For more information about PRETEXT grouping and annotation factors, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_728">

2994PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group Definitions</a>

2995 section.</p>

2996<p id="_2403" tabindex="-1">

2997<strong>

2998Hepatoblastoma prognosis by Evans surgical stage.</strong>

2999 Current study protocols use the PRETEXT staging for prognosis. The historical prognosis, based on Evans stage, is listed below. For more information, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_11">

3000Evans Surgical Staging for Childhood Liver Cancer (Historical)</a>

3001 section.</p>

3002<div class="pdq-content-list">

3003<ul id="_2404">

3004<li>

3005<strong>

3006Stages I and II.</strong>

3007<p id="_520" tabindex="-1">

3008Approximately 20% to 30% of children with hepatoblastoma have stage I or II disease. Prognosis varies depending on the subtype of hepatoblastoma:</p>

3009<div class="pdq-content-list">

3010<ul id="_2405">

3011<li>

3012Patients with well-differentiated fetal (previously termed <em>

3013pure</em>

3014 fetal) histology tumors (4% of hepatoblastomas) have a 3- to 5-year OS rate of 100% with minimal or no chemotherapy, whether PRETEXT I, II, or III.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

301552</a>

3016-<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

301754</a>

3018]</li>

3019<li>

3020Patients with non&#8211;well-differentiated fetal histology, non&#8211;small cell undifferentiated stage I and II hepatoblastomas have a 3- to 4-year OS rate of 90% to 100% with adjuvant chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

302152</a>

3022,<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

302353</a>

3024]</li>

3025<li>

3026If any small cell undifferentiated elements are present in patients with stage I or II hepatoblastoma, the 3-year survival rate is 40% to 70%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

302752</a>

3028,<a href="#cit/section_6.55">

302955</a>

3030]</li>

3031</ul>

3032</div>

3033</li>

3034<li>

3035<strong>

3036Stage III.</strong>

3037<p id="_522" tabindex="-1">

3038Approximately 50% to 70% of children with hepatoblastoma have stage III disease. The 3- to 5-year OS rate for children with stage III hepatoblastoma is less than 70%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

303952</a>

3040,<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

304153</a>

3042]</p>

3043</li>

3044<li>

3045<strong>

3046Stage IV.</strong>

3047<p id="_523" tabindex="-1">

3048Approximately 10% to 20% of children with hepatoblastoma have stage IV disease. The 3- to 5-year OS rate for children with stage IV hepatoblastoma varies widely, from 20% to approximately 60%, based on published reports.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

304952</a>

3050,<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

305153</a>

3052,<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

305356</a>

3054-<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

305559</a>

3056] Postsurgical stage IV is equivalent to any PRETEXT group with annotation factor M.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

305750</a>

3058,<a href="#cit/section_6.60">

305960</a>

3060,<a href="#cit/section_6.61">

306161</a>

3062]</p>

3063</li>

3064</ul>

3065</div>

3066</section>

3067<section id="_2392">

3068<h4 id="_2392_toc">

3069Prognostic factors</h4>

3070<p id="_582" tabindex="-1">

3071Individual childhood cancer study groups have attempted to define the relative importance of a variety of prognostic factors present at diagnosis and in response to therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.62">

307262</a>

3073,<a href="#cit/section_6.63">

307463</a>

3075] The CHIC study group retrospectively combined data from eight clinical trials (N = 1,605) conducted between 1988 and 2010. They published a univariate analysis of the effect of clinical prognostic factors present at the time of diagnosis on event-free survival (EFS).[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

307650</a>

3077,<a href="#cit/section_6.64">

307864</a>

3079] The analysis confirmed many of the statistically significant adverse factors described below:[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

308050</a>

3081]</p>

3082<div class="pdq-content-list">

3083<ul id="_897">

3084<li>

3085<strong>

3086Higher PRETEXT group.</strong>

3087[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

308850</a>

3089]</li>

3090<li>

3091<strong>

3092Positive PRETEXT annotation factors:</strong>

3093[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

309450</a>

3095]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3096<ul id="_1839">

3097<li>

3098V: Involvement of all three hepatic veins and/or intrahepatic inferior vena cava.</li>

3099<li>

3100P: Involvement of both left and right portal veins.</li>

3101<li>

3102E: Contiguous extrahepatic tumor extensions (e.g., diaphragm, adjacent organs).</li>

3103<li>

3104F: Multifocal tumors.</li>

3105<li>

3106R: Tumor rupture.</li>

3107<li>

3108M: Distant metastases, usually lung.</li>

3109</ul>

3110</div>

3111</li>

3112<li>

3113<strong>

3114Low AFP level (&lt;100 ng/mL or 100&#8211;1,000 ng/mL to account for infants with elevated AFP levels).</strong>

3115[<a href="#cit/section_6.64">

311664</a>

3117]</li>

3118<li>

3119<strong>

3120Older age.</strong>

3121 Patients aged 3 to 7 years have a worse outcome in the PRETEXT IV group.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

312250</a>

3123] Patients aged 8 years and older have a worse outcome than do younger patients in all PRETEXT groups. In a subsequent report from the CHIC group, risk of an event increased with advancing age throughout all age cohorts.[<a href="#cit/section_6.65">

312465</a>

3125][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3126Level of evidence C1</a>

3127] Increasing age attenuated the effect of other risk factors, including metastasis, AFP level less than 100 ng/mL, tumor rupture, and the presence of one annotation factor.<p id="_1879" tabindex="-1">

3128 In contrast, in the SIOPEL-2 and -3 studies, infants younger than 6 months had PRETEXT, annotation factors, and outcomes similar to that of older children undergoing the same treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_6.66">

312966</a>

3130][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3131Level of evidence C1</a>

3132]</p>

3133</li>

3134</ul>

3135</div>

3136<p id="_898" tabindex="-1">

3137In the CHIC study, sex, prematurity, birth weight, and Beckwith-Wiedemann syndrome had no effect on EFS.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

313850</a>

3139]</p>

3140<p id="_1880" tabindex="-1">

3141 A multivariate analysis of these prognostic factors was published to help develop a new risk group classification for hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.64">

314264</a>

3143] This classification was used to generate a risk stratification schema to be used in international clinical trials. For more information, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_915">

3144International risk classification model</a>

3145 section.</p>

3146<p id="_777" tabindex="-1">

3147Other studies observed the following factors that affected prognosis:</p>

3148<div class="pdq-content-list">

3149<ul id="_583">

3150<li>

3151<strong>

3152PRETEXT group:</strong>

3153 In SIOPEL studies, having a low PRETEXT group at diagnosis (PRETEXT I, II, and III tumors) is a good prognostic factor, whereas PRETEXT IV is a poor prognostic factor.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

315450</a>

3155] For more information, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_10">

3156Tumor Stratification by Imaging and Evans Surgical Staging for Childhood Liver Cancer</a>

3157 section.</li>

3158<li>

3159<strong>

3160Tumor stage:</strong>

3161 In COG studies, patients with stage I tumors that were resected at diagnosis with usual hepatoblastoma histology have a favorable outcome when treated with limited chemotherapy. Patients with tumors that have well-differentiated fetal histology have an excellent prognosis. These tumors are not generally treated with chemotherapy. Patients with tumors of other stages and histologies are treated more aggressively.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

316250</a>

3163] </li>

3164<li>

3165<strong>

3166Treatment-related factors:</strong>

3167<p id="_365" tabindex="-1">

3168<strong>

3169Chemotherapy:</strong>

3170 Chemotherapy often decreases the size and extent of hepatoblastoma, allowing complete resection.[<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

317153</a>

3172,<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

317356</a>

3174,<a href="#cit/section_6.67">

317567</a>

3176-<a href="#cit/section_6.69">

317769</a>

3178] Favorable response of the primary tumor to chemotherapy, defined as either a 30% decrease in tumor size by Response Evaluation Criteria In Solid Tumors (RECIST) or 90% or greater decrease in AFP levels, predicted the resectability of the tumor. In turn, this favorable response predicted OS among all CHIC risk groups treated with neoadjuvant chemotherapy in the JPLT-2 Japanese national clinical trial.[<a href="#cit/section_6.70">

317970</a>

3180][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3181Level of evidence B4</a>

3182]</p>

3183<p id="_346" tabindex="-1">

3184<strong>

3185Surgery: </strong>

3186Cure of hepatoblastoma requires gross tumor resection. Hepatoblastoma is most often unifocal, so resection may be possible. If a hepatoblastoma is completely removed, most patients survive. However, because of vascular or other involvement, less than one-third of patients have lesions that are amenable to complete resection at

3187diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

318850</a>

3189] It is critically important that a child with probable hepatoblastoma be evaluated by a pediatric surgeon who is experienced in the techniques of extreme liver resection with vascular reconstruction. The child should also have access to a liver transplant program. In advanced tumors, surgical treatment of hepatoblastoma is a demanding procedure. Postoperative complications in high-risk patients decrease the OS rate.[<a href="#cit/section_6.71">

319071</a>

3191]</p>

3192<p id="_1853" tabindex="-1">

3193<strong>

3194Orthotopic liver transplant:</strong>

3195 Orthotopic liver transplant is an additional treatment option for patients whose tumor remains unresectable after preoperative chemotherapy;[<a href="#cit/section_6.51">

319651</a>

3197,<a href="#cit/section_6.72">

319872</a>

3199] however, the presence of microscopic residual tumor at the surgical margin does not preclude a favorable outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

320073</a>

3201,<a href="#cit/section_6.74">

320274</a>

3203] This may result from the additional courses of chemotherapy that are administered before or after resection.[<a href="#cit/section_6.67">

320467</a>

3205,<a href="#cit/section_6.68">

320668</a>

3207,<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

320873</a>

3209] </p>

3210<p id="_738" tabindex="-1">

3211For more information about the outcomes associated with specific chemotherapy regimens, see <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_725">

3212Table 6</a>

3213.</p>

3214</li>

3215<li>

3216<strong>

3217Tumor marker&#8211;related factors:</strong>

3218<p id="_351" tabindex="-1">

3219Ninety percent of children with hepatoblastoma and two-thirds of children with hepatocellular carcinoma exhibit elevated levels of the serum tumor marker AFP, which parallels disease activity. The level of AFP at diagnosis and rate of decrease in AFP levels during treatment are compared with the age-adjusted normal range. Lack of a significant decrease in AFP levels with treatment may

3220predict a poor response to therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.75">

322175</a>

3222] In an exploratory study of 34 children with hepatoblastoma, the rate of decrease in AFP and tumor volume, but not in RECIST I measurements, following two courses of treatment after diagnosis was predictive of EFS and OS.[<a href="#cit/section_6.76">

322376</a>

3224]</p>

3225<p id="_584" tabindex="-1">

3226Absence of elevated AFP

3227levels at diagnosis (AFP &lt;100 ng/mL) occurs in a small percentage of children with hepatoblastoma and appears to be associated with very poor prognosis, as well as with the small cell undifferentiated variant of hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

322850</a>

3229] Some of these variants do not express SMARCB1 and may be considered rhabdoid tumors of the liver, which require alternative therapy. All small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastomas are tested for loss of SMARCB1 expression by immunohistochemistry to determine those that should be treated as a hepatoblastoma versus those that should be treated as rhabdoid tumors of the liver.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

323052</a>

3231,<a href="#cit/section_6.55">

323255</a>

3233,<a href="#cit/section_6.58">

323458</a>

3235,<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

323659</a>

3237,<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

323877</a>

3239,<a href="#cit/section_6.78">

324078</a>

3241]</p>

3242<p id="_352" tabindex="-1">

3243Beta-hCG levels may also be elevated in children with hepatoblastoma or hepatocellular carcinoma, which may result in isosexual precocity in boys.[<a href="#cit/section_6.45">

324445</a>

3245,<a href="#cit/section_6.46">

324646</a>

3247] </p>

3248</li>

3249<li>

3250<strong>

3251Tumor histology:</strong>

3252<p id="_602" tabindex="-1">

3253For more information, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_585">

3254Histology</a>

3255 section in the Hepatoblastoma section.</p>

3256</li>

3257</ul>

3258</div>

3259<p id="_613" tabindex="-1">

3260Other variables have been proposed to be poor prognostic factors, but the relative importance of their prognostic significance has been difficult to define. In the SIOPEL-1 study, a multivariate analysis of prognosis after positive response to chemotherapy showed that only one variable, PRETEXT, predicted OS, while metastasis and PRETEXT predicted EFS.[<a href="#cit/section_6.77">

326177</a>

3262] In an analysis of the U.S. intergroup study from the time of diagnosis, well-differentiated fetal histology, small cell undifferentiated histology, and AFP less than 100 ng/mL were prognostic in a log rank analysis. PRETEXT was prognostic among patients designated group III, but not group IV.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

326352</a>

3264,<a href="#cit/section_6.79">

326579</a>

3266] The CHIC study incorporated detailed hepatoblastoma patient data from multiple groups, establishing a solid foundation of risk factors.[<a href="#cit/section_6.79">

326779</a>

3268]</p>

3269</section>

3270</section>

3271<section id="_585">

3272<h3 id="_585_toc">

3273Histology</h3>

3274<p id="_468" tabindex="-1">

3275Hepatoblastoma arises from precursors of hepatocytes and can have several morphologies, including the following:[<a href="#cit/section_6.80">

327680</a>

3277]</p>

3278<div class="pdq-content-list">

3279<ul id="_469">

3280<li>

3281Small cells that reflect neither epithelial nor stromal differentiation. It is critical to discriminate between small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma expressing SMARCB1 and rhabdoid tumor of the liver, which lacks the <em class="gene-name">

3282SMARCB1</em>

3283 gene and SMARCB1 expression. Both diseases may share similar histology. Optimal treatment of rhabdoid tumor of the liver and small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma may require different approaches and different chemotherapy. For a more extensive discussion on the differences of these two diseases, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_493">

3284Small cell undifferentiated histology hepatoblastoma and rhabdoid tumors of the liver</a>

3285 section.</li>

3286<li>

3287Embryonal epithelial cells resembling the liver epithelium at 6 to 8 weeks of gestation.</li>

3288<li>

3289Well-differentiated fetal hepatocytes morphologically indistinguishable from normal fetal liver cells.</li>

3290</ul>

3291</div>

3292<p id="_411" tabindex="-1">

3293Most often the tumor consists of a mixture of epithelial hepatocyte precursors. About 20% of tumors have stromal derivatives such as osteoid, chondroid, and rhabdoid elements. Occasionally, neuronal, melanocytic, squamous, and enteroendocrine elements are found. The following histological subtypes have clinical relevance:</p>

3294<div class="pdq-content-list">

3295<ul id="_586">

3296<li>

3297<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_517">

3298Well-differentiated fetal (pure fetal) histology hepatoblastoma</a>

3299.</li>

3300<li>

3301Mixed fetal and embryonal epithelial cells.</li>

3302<li>

3303<a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_493">

3304Small cell undifferentiated histology hepatoblastoma and rhabdoid tumors of the liver</a>

3305.<div class="pdq-content-list">

3306<ul id="_1989">

3307<li>

3308Small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma (<em class="gene-name">

3309SMARCB1</em>

3310 positive).</li>

3311<li>

3312Rhabdoid tumor of liver (<em class="gene-name">

3313SMARCB1</em>

3314 negative).</li>

3315</ul>

3316</div>

3317</li>

3318</ul>

3319</div>

3320<section id="_517">

3321<h4 id="_517_toc">

3322Well-differentiated fetal (<em>

3323pure fetal</em>

3324) histology hepatoblastoma</h4>

3325<p id="_518" tabindex="-1">

3326An analysis of patients with initially resected hepatoblastoma tumors (before receiving chemotherapy) has suggested that patients with well-differentiated fetal (previously termed <em>

3327pure fetal</em>

3328) histology tumors have a better prognosis than do patients with an admixture of more primitive and rapidly dividing embryonal components or other undifferentiated tissues. Studies have reported the following:</p>

3329<div class="pdq-content-list">

3330<ol id="_587">

3331<li>

3332A study of patients with hepatoblastoma and well-differentiated fetal histology tumors observed the following:[<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

333353</a>

3334]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3335<ul id="_588">

3336<li>

3337The survival rate was 100% for patients who received four doses of single-agent doxorubicin. This suggested that patients with well-differentiated fetal histology tumors might not need chemotherapy after complete resection.[<a href="#cit/section_6.81">

333881</a>

3339,<a href="#cit/section_6.82">

334082</a>

3341] </li>

3342</ul>

3343</div>

3344</li>

3345<li>

3346In a COG study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00003994">

3347COG-P9645</a>

3348), 16 patients with well-differentiated fetal histology hepatoblastoma with two or fewer mitoses per 10 high-power fields were not treated with chemotherapy. Retrospectively, their PRETEXT groups were group I (n = 4), group II (n = 6), and group III (n = 2).[<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

334954</a>

3350]<div class="pdq-content-list">

3351<ul id="_589">

3352<li>

3353The survival rate was 100% for these patients who did not receive chemotherapy.</li>

3354<li>

3355All 16 patients were alive with no evidence of disease at a median follow-up of 4.9 years (range, 9 months to 9.2 years).</li>

3356</ul>

3357</div>

3358 </li>

3359</ol>

3360</div>

3361<p id="_590" tabindex="-1">

3362Thus, complete resection of a well-differentiated fetal hepatoblastoma may preclude the need for chemotherapy.</p>

3363</section>

3364<section id="_493">

3365<h4 id="_493_toc">

3366Small cell undifferentiated histology hepatoblastoma and rhabdoid tumors of the liver</h4>

3367<p id="_494" tabindex="-1">

3368Small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma (<em class="gene-name">

3369SMARCB1</em>

3370 retained) is an uncommon hepatoblastoma variant. Histologically, small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma is typified by a diffuse population of small cells with scant cytoplasm resembling neuroblasts.[<a href="#cit/section_6.83">

337183</a>

3372] It is now recognized that small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma may be difficult to distinguish from malignant rhabdoid tumor of the liver, which has been conflated with small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma in past studies. </p>

3373<p id="_2428" tabindex="-1">

3374Small cell undifferentiated histology hepatoblastoma and rhabdoid tumors of the livers can be distinguished by the following characteristic abnormalities:</p>

3375<div class="pdq-content-list">

3376<ul id="_592">

3377<li>

3378Chromosomal abnormalities. These abnormalities in rhabdoid tumors include translocations involving a breakpoint on chromosome 22q11 and homozygous deletion at the chromosome 22q12 region that harbors the <em class="gene-name">

3379SMARCB1</em>

3380 gene.[<a href="#cit/section_6.84">

338184</a>

3382,<a href="#cit/section_6.85">

338385</a>

3384]</li>

3385<li>

3386Lack of SMARCB1 expression. Lack of detection of SMARCB1 by immunohistochemistry is characteristic of malignant rhabdoid tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_6.84">

338784</a>

3388]</li>

3389</ul>

3390</div>

3391<p id="_2429" tabindex="-1">

3392Historically, small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma was reported to occur at a younger age (6&#8211;10 months) than other cases of hepatoblastoma [<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

339352</a>

3394,<a href="#cit/section_6.84">

339584</a>

3396] and was associated with AFP levels that are normal for age at presentation.[<a href="#cit/section_6.55">

339755</a>

3398,<a href="#cit/section_6.84">

339984</a>

3400] However, in a prospective study by the Children's Oncology Group (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00980460">

3401AHEP0731 [NCT00980460]</a>

3402), the presence of small cell undifferentiated histology did not correlate with age, sex, or AFP levels at diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.86">

340386</a>

3404]</p>

3405<p id="_1939" tabindex="-1">

3406The ongoing Paediatric Hepatic International Tumour Trial (PHITT) designates any childhood liver tumor as rhabdoid tumor of the liver if it contains cells that lack SMARCB1 expression. Patients with <em class="gene-name">

3407SMARCB1</em>

3408-negative tumors, which are presumed to be related to rhabdoid tumors, may not be enrolled in the international trial, which addresses treatment of hepatoblastoma that includes small cell undifferentiated histology, hepatocellular carcinoma, and hepatic malignancy of childhood, not otherwise specified (NOS), but not rhabdoid tumor of the liver. In this trial, all patients with histology consistent with pure small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma, as assessed by the institutional pathologist, are required to have testing for <em class="gene-name">

3409SMARCB1</em>

3410 by immunohistochemistry according to the practices at the institution. In addition, presence of a blastemal component indicates conventional hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.80">

341180</a>

3412] For more information about the AHEP1531 trial, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_1796">

3413Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation for Hepatoblastoma</a>

3414 section.</p>

3415<p id="_1929" tabindex="-1">

3416A characteristic shared by both small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma and malignant rhabdoid tumor is the poor prognosis associated with each.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

341752</a>

3418,<a href="#cit/section_6.84">

341984</a>

3420,<a href="#cit/section_6.87">

342187</a>

3422] However, because small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma and rhabdoid tumor of the liver have not been discriminated in past studies, some of the prognostic features attributed to the former may have been contributed in part by the latter. Published studies of prognostic features related to small cell undifferentiated histology include the following: </p>

3423<div class="pdq-content-list">

3424<ul id="_1930">

3425<li>

3426In 2009, the results of a study of 11 young children with low AFP levels and small cell morphology were reported. Ten children died of disease progression, and one child died of complications. Six of six children tested were <em class="gene-name">

3427SMARCB1</em>

3428 negative, but only one child had any rhabdoid morphology. This finding suggests that many or all liver tumors with small cell morphology and very low AFP levels in young children may be rhabdoid tumors of the liver. These tumors have a poor prognosis that is associated with the driver mutation.[<a href="#cit/section_6.84">

342984</a>

3430]</li>

3431<li>

3432A single-institution study of seven children with small cell morphology liver tumors found that all retained expression of <em class="gene-name">

3433SMARCB1</em>

3434. Six children survived, and one child died of complications from liver transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_6.88">

343588</a>

3436]</li>

3437<li>

3438A study of 23 liver tumors from the Kiel tumor bank found 12 tumors with small cell morphology. Nine tumors had malignant rhabdoid tumor classic histology, and two tumors had mixed small cell and rhabdoid histologies. Outcomes were not provided, but it was noted that rhabdoid brain tumors had small cell, not classic, rhabdoid histology.[<a href="#cit/section_6.89">

343989</a>

3440]</li>

3441<li>

3442In a single-institution study of six children with <em class="gene-name">

3443SMARCB1</em>

3444-negative liver tumors, two children with small cell morphology died. The remaining four children with classic rhabdoid histology were not treated with cisplatin-based therapy; three children survived, and one child died of complications from transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_6.90">

344590</a>

3446]</li>

3447<li>

3448A report from the COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00980460">

3449AHEP0731 (NCT00980460)</a>

3450 trial identified 35 of 177 evaluable patients (19%) with small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma confirmed by central review.[<a href="#cit/section_6.86">

345186</a>

3452] SMARCB1 nuclear expression was retained in 33 of 35 patients. Unlike previous reports, the presence of small cell undifferentiated histology did not correlate with age, sex, or AFP levels at diagnosis. The 5-year EFS rates for patients with low-, intermediate-, and high-risk small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastoma were 86% (95% CI, 33%&#8211;98%), 81% (95% CI, 51%&#8211;92%), and 29% (95% CI, 4%&#8211;81%), respectively. The 5-year EFS rates for patients with low-, intermediate-, and high-risk hepatoblastoma without small cell undifferentiated histology were 87% (95% CI, 72%&#8211;95%), 88% (95% CI, 79%&#8211;95%), and 55% (95% CI, 33%&#8211;74%); <em>

3453P</em>

3454 = .17), respectively. In this trial, concordance between local and central review was poor, and they agreed in only 9 of 35 cases (26%). All tumors were tested for SMARCB1 expression by immunohistochemistry. In this study, hepatoblastoma that would otherwise be considered very low risk or low risk was upgraded to intermediate risk if any small cell undifferentiated elements were found. For more information, see <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_695">

3455Table 5</a>

3456.</li>

3457</ul>

3458</div>

3459<p id="_1931" tabindex="-1">

3460The outcomes of the CHIC trial of childhood liver tumors may clarify some of the questions regarding these different histological and genetic findings.</p>

3461</section>

3462</section>

3597</table>

3598<section id="_915">

3599<h4 id="_915_toc">

3600International risk classification model</h4>

3601<p id="_916" tabindex="-1">

3602 The CHIC group developed a novel risk stratification system for use in international clinical trials on the basis of prognostic features present at diagnosis. CHIC unified the disparate definitions and staging systems used by pediatric cooperative multicenter trial groups, enabling the comparison of studies conducted by heterogeneous groups in different countries.[<a href="#cit/section_6.64">

360364</a>

3604] Original detailed clinical patient data were extracted from eight published clinical trials using central review of imaging and histology, and prognostic factors were identified by univariate analysis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

360550</a>

3606]</p>

3607<p id="_1881" tabindex="-1">

3608Based on the initial univariate analysis of the data combined with historical clinical treatment patterns and data from previous large clinical trials, five backbone groups were selected, which allowed for further risk stratification. Subsequent multivariate analysis on the basis of these backbone groups defined the following clinical prognostic factors: PRETEXT group (I, II, III, or IV), presence of metastasis (yes or no), and AFP (&#8804;100 ng/mL). The backbone groups are as follows:[<a href="#cit/section_6.64">

360964</a>

3610]</p>

3611<div class="pdq-content-list">

3612<ul id="_930">

3613<li>

3614Backbone 1: PRETEXT I/II, not metastatic, AFP greater than 100 ng/mL.</li>

3615<li>

3616Backbone 2: PRETEXT III, not metastatic, AFP greater than 100 ng/mL.</li>

3617<li>

3618Backbone 3: PRETEXT IV, not metastatic, AFP greater than 100 ng/mL. </li>

3619<li>

3620Backbone 4: Any PRETEXT group, metastatic disease at diagnosis, AFP greater than 100 ng/mL.</li>

3621<li>

3622Backbone 5: Any PRETEXT group, metastatic or not, AFP less than or equal to 100 ng/mL at diagnosis.</li>

3623</ul>

3624</div>

3625<p id="_931" tabindex="-1">

3626Other diagnostic factors (e.g., age) were queried for each of the backbone categories, including the presence of at least one of the following PRETEXT annotations (defined as VPEFR+, see <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_1907">

3627Table 2</a>

3628) or AFP less than or equal to 100 ng/mL:[<a href="#cit/section_6.64">

362964</a>

3630] </p>

3631<div class="pdq-content-list">

3632<ul id="_932">

3633<li>

3634V: Involvement of vena cava or all three hepatic veins, or both.</li>

3635<li>

3636P: Involvement of portal bifurcation or both right and left portal veins, or both.</li>

3637<li>

3638E: Extrahepatic contiguous tumor extension.</li>

3639<li>

3640F: Multifocal liver tumor.</li>

3641<li>

3642R: Tumor rupture at diagnosis.</li>

3643</ul>

3644</div>

3645<p id="_933" tabindex="-1">

3646An assessment of surgical resectability at diagnosis was added for PRETEXT I and II patients. Patients in each of the five backbone categories were stratified on the basis of backwards stepwise elimination multivariable analysis of additional patient characteristics, including age and presence or absence of PRETEXT annotation factors (V, P, E, F, and R). Each of these subcategories received one of four risk designations (very low, low, intermediate, or high). The result of the multivariate analysis was used to assign patients to very low-, low-, intermediate-, and high-risk categories, as shown in <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_917">

3647Figure 2</a>

3648. For example, the finding of an AFP level of 100 to 1,000 ng/mL was significant only among patients younger than 8 years in the backbone PRETEXT III group. The analysis enables prognostically similar risk groups to be assigned to the appropriate treatment groups on upcoming international protocols.[<a href="#cit/section_6.64">

364964</a>

3650]</p>

3651<p id="_917" tabindex="-1">

3652<figure id="figure_918" class="image-center">

3653<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/785619.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

3654Enlarge</a>

3655<img id="_918" alt="Diagram showing risk stratification trees for the Children&#8217;s Hepatic tumors International Collaboration&#8212;Hepatoblastoma Stratification (CHIC-HS)." title="Diagram showing risk stratification trees for the Children&#8217;s Hepatic tumors International Collaboration&#8212;Hepatoblastoma Stratification (CHIC-HS)." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/785619-750.jpg">

3656<figcaption class="caption-container">

3657Figure 2. Risk stratification trees for the Children&#8217;s Hepatic tumors International Collaboration&#8212;Hepatoblastoma Stratification (CHIC-HS). Very low-risk group and low-risk group are separated only by their resectability at diagnosis, which has been defined by international consensus as part of the surgical guidelines for the upcoming collaborative trial, Paediatric Hepatic International Tumour Trial (PHITT). Separate risk stratification trees are used for each of the four PRETEXT groups. AFP = alpha-fetoprotein. M = metastatic disease. PRETEXT = PRETreatment EXTent of disease.<a href="#cit/citation.64">

365864</a>

3659 Reprinted from <a href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/journal/14702045" title="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/journal/14702045">

3660The Lancet Oncology</a>

3661, Volume 18, Meyers RL, Maibach R, Hiyama E, H&#228;berle B, Krailo M, Rangaswami A, Aronson DC, Malogolowkin MH, Perilongo G, von Schweinitz D, Ansari M, Lopez-Terrada D, Tanaka Y, Alaggio R, Leuschner I, Hishiki T, Schmid I, Watanabe K, Yoshimura K, Feng Y, Rinaldi E, Saraceno D, Derosa M, Czauderna P, Risk-stratified staging in paediatric hepatoblastoma: a unified analysis from the Children's Hepatic tumors International Collaboration, Pages 122&#8211;131, Copyright (2017), with permission from Elsevier.</figcaption>

3662</figure>

3663</p>

3664</section>

3665</section>

3666<section id="_373">

3667<h3 id="_373_toc">

3668Treatment of Hepatoblastoma </h3>

3669<p id="_1882" tabindex="-1">

3670Treatment options for newly diagnosed hepatoblastoma depend on the following:</p>

3671<div class="pdq-content-list">

3672<ul id="_1883">

3673<li>

3674Whether the cancer is resectable at diagnosis.</li>

3675<li>

3676The tumor histology.</li>

3677<li>

3678How the cancer responds to chemotherapy.</li>

3679<li>

3680Whether the cancer has metastasized.</li>

3681</ul>

3682</div>

3683<p id="_767" tabindex="-1">

3684Cisplatin-based chemotherapy has resulted in a survival rate of more than 90% for children with PRETEXT and POST-Treatment EXTent (POSTTEXT) I and II resectable disease before or after chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

368556</a>

3686,<a href="#cit/section_6.58">

368758</a>

3688,<a href="#cit/section_6.68">

368968</a>

3690]</p>

3691<p id="_720" tabindex="-1">

3692Chemotherapy regimens used in the treatment of hepatoblastoma and their respective outcomes are described in <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_725">

3693Table 6</a>

3694. For information describing each stage, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_10">

3695Tumor Stratification by Imaging and Evans Surgical Staging for Childhood Liver Cancer</a>

3696 section.</p>
