4119<section id="_240">

4120<h4 id="_240_toc">

4121Treatment options for hepatoblastoma that is resectable at diagnosis</h4>

4122<p id="_651" tabindex="-1">

4123Approximately 20% to 30% of children with hepatoblastoma have resectable disease at diagnosis. COG surgical guidelines (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00980460">

4124AHEP0731 [NCT00980460]</a>

4125 appendix) recommend tumor resection at diagnosis without preoperative chemotherapy in children with PRETEXT I tumors and PRETEXT II tumors with greater than 1 cm radiographic margin on the vena cava and middle hepatic and portal veins. Outcomes for patients after undergoing a complete resection at diagnosis, compared with patients who had positive microscopic margins found at resection, are similar after receiving chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.58">

412658</a>

4127,<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

412859</a>

4129,<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

413073</a>

4131]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.95">

413295</a>

4133][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4134Level of evidence C1</a>

4135]</p>

4136<p id="_1841" tabindex="-1">

4137Prognosis varies depending on the histological subtype, as follows:</p>

4138<div class="pdq-content-list">

4139<ul id="_1872">

4140<li>

4141Patients with well-differentiated fetal histology (4% of hepatoblastomas) have a 3- to 5-year OS rate of 100% with minimal or no adjuvant chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

414252</a>

4143-<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

414454</a>

4145,<a href="#cit/section_6.96">

414696</a>

4147]</li>

4148<li>

4149Patients with non&#8211;well-differentiated fetal histology, non&#8211;small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastomas have a 3- to 4-year OS rate of 90% to 100% with adjuvant chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

415052</a>

4151,<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

415253</a>

4153,<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

415456</a>

4155,<a href="#cit/section_6.58">

415658</a>

4157,<a href="#cit/section_6.97">

415897</a>

4159]</li>

4160<li>

4161If any small cell undifferentiated elements are present, the 3-year survival rate is 40% to 70%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.52">

416252</a>

4163,<a href="#cit/section_6.55">

416455</a>

4165]</li>

4166</ul>

4167</div>

4168<p id="_646" tabindex="-1">

4169<strong>

4170Treatment options for hepatoblastoma resectable at diagnosis showing non&#8211;well-differentiated fetal histology include the following:</strong>

4171</p>

4172<div class="pdq-content-list">

4173<ol id="_647">

4174<li>

4175Resection followed by two to four cycles of chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.50">

417650</a>

4177]</li>

4178</ol>

4179</div>

4180<p id="_1884" tabindex="-1">

4181Re-resection of positive microscopic margins may not be necessary. Conclusive evidence is lacking for tumors with resection at diagnosis compared with those with positive microscopic margins resected after preoperative chemotherapy.</p>

4182<p id="_648" tabindex="-1">

4183Evidence (gross surgical resection, with or without microscopic margins, and postoperative chemotherapy):</p>

4184<div class="pdq-content-list">

4185<ol id="_649">

4186<li>

4187In the COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00980460">

4188AHEP0731 (NCT00980460)</a>

4189 trial, 49 of 51 patients with stage I or stage II hepatoblastoma (without pure fetal histology) received two cycles of adjuvant chemotherapy consisting of cisplatin, fluorouracil, and vincristine.[<a href="#cit/section_6.93">

419093</a>

4191][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4192Level of evidence C1</a>

4193] <div class="pdq-content-list">

4194<ul id="_1973">

4195<li>

4196The 5-year EFS rate was 88%, and the 5-year OS rate was 91%.</li>

4197<li>

4198This outcome is comparable to the outcomes for children treated with four cycles after initial resection, and to the outcomes for children treated with two cycles of neoadjuvant chemotherapy before resection followed by two cycles of chemotherapy after resection.</li>

4199</ul>

4200</div>

4201</li>

4202<li>

4203There is no reliable data for local recurrence risk in patients with a positive microscopic margin status who underwent resection at diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_6.69">

420469</a>

4205] SIOPEL studies suggest that in patients who received preoperative chemotherapy, positive microscopic margin did not increase risk of local recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_6.58">

420658</a>

4207,<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

420859</a>

4209,<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

421073</a>

4211]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.95">

421295</a>

4213][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4214Level of evidence C1</a>

4215]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4216<ul id="_1855">

4217<li>

4218In a European study conducted between 1990 and 1994, 11 patients had tumor found at the surgical margins after hepatic resection and two patients died, neither of whom had a local recurrence. None of the 11 patients underwent a second resection, and only one patient received radiation therapy postoperatively. All of the patients were treated with four courses of cisplatin and doxorubicin before surgery and received two courses of postoperative chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

421973</a>

4220]</li>

4221<li>

4222In another European study of high-risk hepatoblastoma, 11 patients had microscopic residual tumor remaining after initial surgery and received two to four postoperative cycles of chemotherapy with no additional surgery. Of these 11 patients, 9 survived.[<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

422359</a>

4224]</li>

4225<li>

4226In the SIOPEL-2 study, 13 of 13 patients with microscopic positive resection margins survived.[<a href="#cit/section_6.58">

422758</a>

4228]</li>

4229<li>

4230An unplanned retrospective study of the SIOPEL-2 and SIOPEL-3 trials found that after four courses of cisplatin for standard-risk patients and seven courses of cisplatin alternating with doxorubicin/carboplatin for high-risk patients, resection was performed where imaging suggested it would be safe. Of the 431 children treated in these trials, 58 patients had positive microscopic tumor margins, and 371 patients were in complete remission. There were no statistically significant differences in the rates of local recurrence, EFS, or OS between the two groups.[<a href="#cit/section_6.95">

423195</a>

4232][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4233Level of evidence C1</a>

4234]</li>

4235</ul>

4236</div>

4237</li>

4238<li>

4239A randomized clinical trial demonstrated comparable efficacy with postoperative cisplatin/vincristine/fluorouracil and cisplatin/doxorubicin in the treatment of patients with hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

424053</a>

4241]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4242<ul id="_650">

4243<li>

4244Although survival outcomes were nominally higher for the children who received cisplatin/doxorubicin, this difference was not statistically significant.</li>

4245<li>

4246The combination of cisplatin/vincristine/fluorouracil was significantly less toxic than were the doses of cisplatin/doxorubicin.</li>

4247</ul>

4248</div>

4249</li>

4250</ol>

4251</div>

4252<p id="_769" tabindex="-1">

4253Results of chemotherapy clinical trials are described in <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_725">

4254Table 6</a>

4255.</p>

4256<p id="_760" tabindex="-1">

4257<strong>

4258Treatment options for hepatoblastoma of well-differentiated fetal (<em>

4259pure fetal</em>

4260) histology resectable at diagnosis include the following:</strong>

4261</p>

4262<div class="pdq-content-list">

4263<ol id="_643">

4264<li>

4265Complete surgical resection followed by watchful waiting or chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

426654</a>

4267]</li>

4268</ol>

4269</div>

4270<p id="_644" tabindex="-1">

4271Evidence (complete surgical resection followed by watchful waiting or chemotherapy):</p>

4272<div class="pdq-content-list">

4273<ol id="_645">

4274<li>

4275In a COG prospective clinical trial (INT0098), nine children with stage I (completely resected) well-differentiated fetal histology and fewer than two mitoses per high-power field were treated with four cycles of adjuvant doxorubicin.[<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

427653</a>

4277]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4278<ul id="_1842">

4279<li>

4280At a median follow-up of 5.1 years, the EFS and OS rates were 100% for all nine children.</li>

4281</ul>

4282</div>

4283</li>

4284<li>

4285In the COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00003994">

4286P9645 (NCT00003994)</a>

4287 study, 16 patients with stage I (completely resected) tumors had well-differentiated fetal histology and received no adjuvant chemotherapy. In a retrospective PRETEXT classification of 21 of these 25 patients with adequate data, PRETEXT I, II, and III tumors were found in 7, 10, and 4 patients, respectively.[<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

428854</a>

4289]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4290<ul id="_1843">

4291<li>

4292The EFS and OS rates were 100% for patients with stage I well-differentiated fetal histology, including one patient who had a second surgery to address a positive tumor margin.</li>

4293</ul>

4294</div>

4295</li>

4296</ol>

4297</div>

4298</section>

4299<section id="_249">

4300<h4 id="_249_toc">

4301Treatment options for hepatoblastoma that is not resectable or not resected at diagnosis</h4>

4302<p id="_1846" tabindex="-1">

4303Approximately 70% to 80% of children with hepatoblastoma have tumors that are not resected at diagnosis. COG surgical guidelines (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00980460">

4304AHEP0731 [NCT00980460]</a>

4305 appendix) recommend a diagnostic biopsy without an attempt to resect the tumor in children with PRETEXT II tumors with less than 1 cm radiographic margin on the vena cava and middle hepatic vein and in all children with PRETEXT III and IV tumors.</p>

4306<p id="_654" tabindex="-1">

4307Treatment options for hepatoblastoma that is not resectable or is not resected at diagnosis include the following:</p>

4308<div class="pdq-content-list">

4309<ol id="_655">

4310<li>

4311Chemotherapy followed by reassessment of surgical resectability and complete surgical resection.</li>

4312<li>

4313Chemotherapy followed by reassessment of surgical resectability and orthotopic liver transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_6.51">

431451</a>

4315,<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

431656</a>

4317,<a href="#cit/section_6.98">

431898</a>

4319-<a href="#cit/section_6.103">

4320103</a>

4321]</li>

4322<li>

4323Transarterial chemoembolization (TACE) and transarterial radioembolization (TARE). TACE and TARE may be used to improve resectability before definitive surgical approaches.[<a href="#cit/section_6.104">

4324104</a>

4325-<a href="#cit/section_6.106">

4326106</a>

4327]</li>

4328</ol>

4329</div>

4330<p id="_770" tabindex="-1">

4331Tumor rupture at presentation, resulting in major hemorrhage that can be controlled by transcatheter arterial embolization or partial resection to stabilize the patient, does not preclude a favorable outcome when followed by chemotherapy and definitive surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_6.107">

4332107</a>

4333]</p>

4334<p id="_225" tabindex="-1">

4335In recent years, most children with hepatoblastoma have been treated with chemotherapy. In European cancer centers, children with resectable hepatoblastoma at diagnosis are treated with preoperative chemotherapy, which may reduce the incidence of surgical complications at the time of resection.[<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

433656</a>

4337,<a href="#cit/section_6.58">

433858</a>

4339,<a href="#cit/section_6.73">

434073</a>

4341] Treatment with preoperative chemotherapy has been shown to benefit children with hepatoblastoma. In contrast, an American intergroup study of treatment of children with hepatoblastoma encouraged resection at the time of diagnosis for all tumors amenable to resection without undue risk. The study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00003994">

4342COG-P9645</a>

4343) did not treat children with stage I tumors of well-differentiated fetal histology with preoperative or postoperative chemotherapy unless they developed progressive disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

434454</a>

4345] In this study, most patients with PRETEXT III and all PRETEXT IV tumors were treated with chemotherapy before resection or transplant.</p>

4346<p id="_745" tabindex="-1">

4347Patients whose tumors remain unresectable after chemotherapy should be considered for liver transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_6.51">

434851</a>

4349,<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

435056</a>

4351,<a href="#cit/section_6.98">

435298</a>

4353-<a href="#cit/section_6.102">

4354102</a>

4355] In the presence of features predicting unresectability, early coordination with a pediatric liver transplant service is critical.[<a href="#cit/section_6.78">

435678</a>

4357] In the COG <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00980460">

4358AHEP0731 (NCT00980460)</a>

4359 study, early referral (i.e., based on imaging done <strong>

4360after</strong>

4361 the second cycle of chemotherapy) to a liver specialty center with transplant capability was recommended for patients with POSTTEXT III tumors with positive V or P and POSTTEXT IV tumors with positive F.</p>

4362<p id="_656" tabindex="-1">

4363Evidence (chemotherapy followed by reassessment of surgical resectability and complete surgical resection or liver transplant):</p>

4364<div class="pdq-content-list">

4365<ol id="_636">

4366<li>

4367In the SIOPEL-1 study, preoperative chemotherapy (doxorubicin and cisplatin) was given to all children with hepatoblastoma with or without metastases. After chemotherapy, and excluding those who underwent a liver transplant (&lt;5% of patients), complete resection was performed.[<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

436856</a>

4369]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4370<ul id="_637">

4371<li>

4372The chemotherapy was well tolerated. </li>

4373<li>

4374Complete resection was obtained in 87% of children.</li>

4375<li>

4376This strategy resulted in an OS rate of 75% at 5 years after diagnosis.</li>

4377</ul>

4378</div>

4379</li>

4380<li>

4381Identical results were seen in a follow-up international study (SIOPEL-2).[<a href="#cit/section_6.58">

438258</a>

4383]</li>

4384<li>

4385The SIOPEL-3 study compared cisplatin alone with cisplatin and doxorubicin in patients with preoperative standard-risk hepatoblastoma. Standard risk was defined as tumor confined to the liver and not involving more than three sectors.[<a href="#cit/section_6.97">

438697</a>

4387][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4388Level of evidence A1</a>

4389]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4390<ul id="_638">

4391<li>

4392 The resection rates and OS rates were similar for the cisplatin (95%) and cisplatin/doxorubicin (93%) groups.</li>

4393</ul>

4394</div>

4395</li>

4396<li>

4397In a pilot study, SIOPEL-3HR, cisplatin alternating with carboplatin/doxorubicin was administered in a dose-intensive fashion to high-risk patients with hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

439859</a>

4399] <div class="pdq-content-list">

4400<ul id="_639">

4401<li>

4402In 74 patients with PRETEXT IV tumors, 22 of whom also had metastases, 31 patients had tumors that became resectable, and 26 patients underwent transplant. The 3-year OS rate of this group was 69% (&#177; 11%). </li>

4403<li>

4404Of the 70 patients with metastases enrolled in the trial, the 3-year EFS rate was 56%, and the OS rate was 62%. Of patients with lung metastases, 50% were able to achieve complete remission of metastases with chemotherapy alone (without lung surgery).</li>

4405</ul>

4406</div>

4407</li>

4408<li>

4409<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00077389">

4410SIOPEL-4 (NCT00077389)</a>

4411 was a multinational feasibility trial of dose-dense cisplatin/doxorubicin chemotherapy and radical surgery for a group of children with high-risk hepatoblastoma. Surgical removal of all remaining tumor lesions after chemotherapy was performed if feasible (including liver transplant and metastasectomy, if needed). Patients whose tumors were resected or whose livers were transplanted after three cycles of chemotherapy subsequently received two postoperative cycles of carboplatin and doxorubicin. Patients whose tumors remained unresectable after three cycles of chemotherapy received two cycles of very intensive carboplatin and doxorubicin before surgery. The primary tumor masses were identified as PRETEXT II (27%), III (44%), and IV (26%).[<a href="#cit/section_6.74">

441274</a>

4413][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4414Level of evidence B4</a>

4415]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4416<ul id="_1936">

4417<li>

4418Ninety-seven percent of patients (60 of 61) had a partial response with chemotherapy.</li>

4419<li>

4420Eighty-five percent of patients (53) underwent complete macroscopic resection; tumor was microscopically present in five patients, all of whom are disease-free survivors.</li>

4421<li>

4422Two patients died postoperatively.</li>

4423<li>

4424There were 37 partial hepatectomies and 16 liver transplants.</li>

4425<li>

4426The study had a total of 62 high-risk patients; 74% of patients (62%&#8211;84%) underwent resection. <div class="pdq-content-list">

4427<ul id="_2413">

4428<li>

4429The 3-year disease-free survival (DFS) rate was 76% (95% CI, 65%&#8211;87%). </li>

4430<li>

4431The 3-year OS rate was 83% (95% CI, 73%&#8211;93%).</li>

4432</ul>

4433</div>

4434</li>

4435<li>

4436Of the 16 PRETEXT IV patients, 11 were downstaged after chemotherapy&#8212;6 patients to PRETEXT III, 4 patients to PRETEXT II, and 1 patient to PRETEXT I. Twelve tumors became resectable; of these, four patients underwent a partial hepatectomy and eight patients underwent a liver transplant. For patients who presented with PRETEXT IV disease:<div class="pdq-content-list">

4437<ul id="_2414">

4438<li>

4439 The 3-year DFS rate was 73% (95% CI, 51%&#8211;96%). </li>

4440<li>

4441The 3-year OS rate was 80% (95% CI, 60%&#8211;100%).</li>

4442</ul>

4443</div>

4444</li>

4445</ul>

4446</div>

4447</li>

4448<li>

4449In approximately 75% of children and adolescents with initially unresectable hepatoblastoma, tumors can be rendered resectable with cisplatin-based preoperative chemotherapy, and 60% to 65% of patients will survive disease-free.[<a href="#cit/section_6.108">

4450108</a>

4451]</li>

4452</ol>

4453</div>

4454<p id="_1885" tabindex="-1">

4455In the United States, unresectable tumors have been treated with chemotherapy before resection or transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

445653</a>

4457,<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

445854</a>

4459,<a href="#cit/section_6.67">

446067</a>

4461,<a href="#cit/section_6.68">

446268</a>

4463] On the basis of radiographic imaging, most stage III and IV hepatoblastomas are rendered resectable after two cycles of chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.109">

4464109</a>

4465] A combination of ifosfamide, cisplatin, and doxorubicin followed by postinduction resection has also been used in the treatment of advanced-stage disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.110">

4466110</a>

4467] Some centers have also used extended resection of selected POSTTEXT III and IV tumors rather than liver transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_6.78">

446878</a>

4469,<a href="#cit/section_6.111">

4470111</a>

4471-<a href="#cit/section_6.114">

4472114</a>

4473] Other options, such as TARE and TACE, have been used to shrink residual tumor mass. TARE may also facilitate surgical resection by tumor shrinkage when added to chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.106">

4474106</a>

4475]</p>

4476<p id="_2415" tabindex="-1">

4477The COG conducted a single-arm phase III trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00980460">

4478AHEP0731 [NCT00980460]</a>

4479) for patients with intermediate-risk hepatoblastoma. The study included 93 patients with unresectable nonmetastatic disease and 9 patients with a complete resection at diagnosis. All of the tumors had small cell undifferentiated histology. The addition of doxorubicin to standard treatment (cisplatin, fluorouracil, and vincristine) was assessed for feasibility and efficacy. In the 93 patients with initially unresectable disease, the 5-year EFS rate was 85% (95% confidence interval [CI], 79%&#8211;93%), and the OS rate was 95% (95% CI, 87%&#8211;98%).[<a href="#cit/section_6.115">

4480115</a>

4481]

4482</p>

4483<p id="_771" tabindex="-1">

4484Chemotherapy followed by TACE, then high-intensity focused ultrasound, showed promising results in China for patients with PRETEXT III and IV tumors, some of which were resectable, but patients did not undergo surgical resection because of parent refusal.[<a href="#cit/section_6.116">

4485116</a>

4486]</p>

4487</section>

4488<section id="_260">

4489<h4 id="_260_toc">

4490Treatment options for hepatoblastoma with metastases at diagnosis</h4>

4491<p id="_261" tabindex="-1">

4492The outcomes of patients with metastatic hepatoblastoma at diagnosis are poor, but long-term survival and cure are possible.[<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

449353</a>

4494,<a href="#cit/section_6.67">

449567</a>

4496,<a href="#cit/section_6.68">

449768</a>

4498] Survival rates at 3 to 5 years range from 20% to 79%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.57">

449957</a>

4500,<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

450159</a>

4502,<a href="#cit/section_6.74">

450374</a>

4504,<a href="#cit/section_6.117">

4505117</a>

4506] To date, the best outcomes for children with metastatic hepatoblastoma resulted from treatment with dose-dense cisplatin and doxorubicin, although significant toxicity was also noted (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00077389">

4507SIOPEL-4 [NCT00077389]</a>

4508 trial).[<a href="#cit/section_6.74">

450974</a>

4510][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4511Level of evidence B4</a>

4512]</p>

4513<p id="_661" tabindex="-1">

4514Treatment options for hepatoblastoma with metastases at diagnosis include the following:</p>

4515<div class="pdq-content-list">

4516<ol id="_662">

4517<li>

4518Chemotherapy followed by reassessment of surgical resectability. <div class="pdq-content-list">

4519<ul id="_780">

4520<li>

4521If the primary tumor and extrahepatic disease (usually pulmonary nodules) are resectable after chemotherapy, surgical resection is followed by additional chemotherapy.</li>

4522<li>

4523If extrahepatic metastatic disease is in complete remission after chemotherapy and/or surgical resection of lung nodule but the primary tumor remains unresectable, orthotopic liver transplant is warranted.</li>

4524</ul>

4525</div>

4526</li>

4527<li>

4528 If extrahepatic metastatic disease is not resectable or the patient is not a transplant candidate, additional chemotherapy, TACE, TARE, or radiation therapy may be indicated.[<a href="#cit/section_6.106">

4529106</a>

4530]</li>

4531</ol>

4532</div>

4533<p id="_663" tabindex="-1">

4534The standard combination chemotherapy regimen in North America is four courses of cisplatin/vincristine/fluorouracil [<a href="#cit/section_6.53">

453553</a>

4536] or doxorubicin/cisplatin,[<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

453754</a>

4538,<a href="#cit/section_6.56">

453956</a>

4540,<a href="#cit/section_6.57">

454157</a>

4542] followed by attempted complete tumor resection. If the tumor is completely removed, two postoperative courses of the same chemotherapy are usually given. Study results for different chemotherapy regimens have been reported. For more information, see <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_725">

4543Table 6</a>

4544.</p>

4545<p id="_747" tabindex="-1">

4546 High-dose chemotherapy with stem cell rescue does not appear to be more effective than standard multiagent chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.118">

4547118</a>

4548]

4549</p>

4550<p id="_664" tabindex="-1">

4551Evidence (chemotherapy to treat metastatic disease at diagnosis followed by surgery):</p>

4552<div class="pdq-content-list">

4553<ol id="_1937">

4554<li>

4555A subset of 39 patients presenting with metastases were entered on the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00077389">

4556SIOPEL-4 (NCT00077389)</a>

4557 trial, a multinational feasibility trial of dose-dense cisplatin/doxorubicin chemotherapy and radical surgery for a group of children with high-risk hepatoblastoma. Patients whose tumors were resected or livers transplanted after three cycles of chemotherapy subsequently received two postoperative cycles of carboplatin and doxorubicin. Patients whose tumors were unresectable after three cycles of chemotherapy received two additional cycles of very intensive carboplatin and doxorubicin before surgery.[<a href="#cit/section_6.74">

455874</a>

4559][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4560Level of evidence B4</a>

4561]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4562<ul id="_1938">

4563<li>

4564After three cycles of chemotherapy, there was a complete response (only in the metastases) in 20 of 39 patients and a partial response in 18 of 39 patients. Nineteen of the patients who achieved a complete response were alive without disease 3 years after diagnosis.</li>

4565<li>

4566Of the patients who achieved a partial response, seven patients underwent metastasectomy near the time of resection or liver transplant, with an OS rate of 100%. An additional seven patients with residual small pulmonary nodules underwent resection without metastasectomy; of those, six patients did well and one patient had a recurrence.</li>

4567<li>

4568Two patients with initial metastases eventually experienced a recurrence.</li>

4569<li>

4570Liver transplant, rather than resection alone, was needed to treat 7 of the 39 patients who presented with metastases.</li>

4571<li>

4572For the subset of 39 patients presenting with metastases, the 3-year DFS rate was 77% (95% CI, 63%&#8211;90%), and the OS rate was 79% (95% CI, 66%&#8211;92%).</li>

4573</ul>

4574</div>

4575</li>

4576</ol>

4577</div>

4578<p id="_671" tabindex="-1">

4579In patients with resected primary tumors, any remaining pulmonary metastases should be surgically removed, if possible.[<a href="#cit/section_6.57">

458057</a>

4581] Resection of pulmonary metastases may be facilitated by computed tomography needle localization or preoperative indocyanine green administration with intraoperative fluorescence localization.[<a href="#cit/section_6.119">

4582119</a>

4583] A review of patients treated on a U.S. intergroup trial suggested that resection of metastasis may be done at the time of resection of the primary tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_6.117">

4584117</a>

4585][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4586Level of evidence C1</a>

4587]</p>

4588<p id="_672" tabindex="-1">

4589If extrahepatic disease is in complete remission after chemotherapy, and the primary tumor remains unresectable, an orthotopic liver transplant may be performed.[<a href="#cit/section_6.54">

459054</a>

4591,<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

459259</a>

4593,<a href="#cit/section_6.74">

459474</a>

4595,<a href="#cit/section_6.110">

4596110</a>

4597] </p>

4598<p id="_748" tabindex="-1">

4599The outcome results are discrepant for patients with lung metastases at diagnosis who undergo orthotopic liver transplant after complete resolution of lung disease in response to pretransplant chemotherapy. Some studies have reported favorable outcomes for these patients,[<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

460059</a>

4601,<a href="#cit/section_6.74">

460274</a>

4603,<a href="#cit/section_6.102">

4604102</a>

4605,<a href="#cit/section_6.110">

4606110</a>

4607] while others have noted high rates of hepatoblastoma recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_6.51">

460851</a>

4609,<a href="#cit/section_6.98">

461098</a>

4611,<a href="#cit/section_6.101">

4612101</a>

4613,<a href="#cit/section_6.104">

4614104</a>

4615] All of these studies are limited by small patient numbers. Additional studies are needed to better define outcomes for this subset of patients. Recent clinical trials have resulted in few pulmonary recurrences in children who presented with metastatic disease and underwent liver transplants.[<a href="#cit/section_6.59">

461659</a>

4617,<a href="#cit/section_6.60">

461860</a>

4619,<a href="#cit/section_6.74">

462074</a>

4621]</p>

4622<p id="_675" tabindex="-1">

4623If extrahepatic disease is not resectable after chemotherapy or the patient is not a transplant candidate, alternative treatment approaches include the following:</p>

4624<div class="pdq-content-list">

4625<ul id="_268">

4626<li>

4627Other chemotherapy agents. Chemotherapy agents such as irinotecan, high-dose cisplatin/etoposide, or continuous-infusion doxorubicin have been used.[<a href="#cit/section_6.120">

4628120</a>

4629-<a href="#cit/section_6.122">

4630122</a>

4631]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.123">

4632123</a>

4633][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4634Level of evidence C1</a>

4635]</li>

4636<li>

4637TACE.[<a href="#cit/section_6.105">

4638105</a>

4639,<a href="#cit/section_6.124">

4640124</a>

4641]</li>

4642<li>

4643Radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.125">

4644125</a>

4645]</li>

4646</ul>

4647</div>

4648</section>

4649</section>

4650<section id="_319">

4651<h3 id="_319_toc">

4652Treatment of Progressive or Recurrent Hepatoblastoma</h3>

4653<p id="_320" tabindex="-1">

4654The prognosis for a patient with progressive or recurrent hepatoblastoma depends on several factors, including the following:[<a href="#cit/section_6.126">

4655126</a>

4656] </p>

4657<div class="pdq-content-list">

4658<ul id="_679">

4659<li>

4660Site of recurrence.</li>

4661<li>

4662Previous treatment.</li>

4663<li>

4664Individual patient considerations. </li>

4665</ul>

4666</div>

4667<p id="_680" tabindex="-1">

4668Treatment options for progressive or recurrent hepatoblastoma include the following:</p>

4669<div class="pdq-content-list">

4670<ol id="_681">

4671<li>

4672<strong>

4673Surgical resection.</strong>

4674 In patients with hepatoblastoma that is completely resected at initial diagnosis, aggressive surgical treatment of isolated pulmonary metastases that develop in the course of the disease, in conjunction with an overall strategy that includes chemotherapy, may make extended DFS possible.[<a href="#cit/section_6.117">

4675117</a>

4676,<a href="#cit/section_6.126">

4677126</a>

4678,<a href="#cit/section_6.127">

4679127</a>

4680]<p id="_682" tabindex="-1">

4681If possible, isolated metastases are resected completely in patients whose primary tumor is controlled.[<a href="#cit/section_6.128">

4682128</a>

4683] A retrospective study of patients in SIOPEL studies 1, 2, and 3 showed a 12% incidence of recurrence after complete remission by imaging and AFP levels. Outcome after recurrence was best if the tumor was amenable to surgery. Of patients who underwent chemotherapy and surgery, the 3-year EFS rate was 34%, and the OS rate was 43%.[<a href="#cit/section_6.126">

4684126</a>

4685][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4686Level of evidence C1</a>

4687] </p>

4688<p id="_683" tabindex="-1">

4689Enrollment in a clinical trial should be considered if all of the recurrent disease cannot be surgically removed. Phase I and phase II clinical trials may be appropriate.</p>

4690</li>

4691<li>

4692<strong>

4693Chemotherapy.</strong>

4694 Analysis of survival after recurrence demonstrated that some patients treated with cisplatin/vincristine/fluorouracil could be salvaged with doxorubicin-containing regimens, but patients treated with doxorubicin/cisplatin could not be salvaged with vincristine/fluorouracil.[<a href="#cit/section_6.129">

4695129</a>

4696] The addition of doxorubicin to vincristine/fluorouracil/cisplatin was clinically evaluated in the COG study <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00980460">

4697AHEP0731 (NCT00980460)</a>

4698. <p id="_2398" tabindex="-1">

4699Combined vincristine/irinotecan and single-agent irinotecan have been used with some success.[<a href="#cit/section_6.123">

4700123</a>

4701]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.122">

4702122</a>

4703][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4704Level of evidence C1</a>

4705]</p>

4706<p id="_1848" tabindex="-1">

4707A review of COG phase I and II studies found no promising agents for relapsed hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.130">

4708130</a>

4709] </p>

4710</li>

4711<li>

4712<strong>

4713Liver transplant.</strong>

4714 Liver transplant should be considered for patients with nonmetastatic disease recurrence in the liver that is not amenable to resection.[<a href="#cit/section_6.51">

471551</a>

4716,<a href="#cit/section_6.98">

471798</a>

4718,<a href="#cit/section_6.101">

4719101</a>

4720] </li>

4721<li>

4722<strong>

4723Percutaneous ablation.</strong>

4724 Percutaneous radiofrequency ablation has been used as an alternative to surgical resection of oligometastatic hepatoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.131">

4725131</a>

4726][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4727Level of evidence C1</a>

4728] Percutaneous ablation techniques may also be considered for palliation.[<a href="#cit/section_6.132">

4729132</a>

4730]</li>

4731</ol>

4732</div>

4733</section>

4734<section id="_1796">

4735<h3 id="_1796_toc">

4736Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation for Hepatoblastoma</h3>

4737<p id="_1797" tabindex="-1">

4738Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

4739NCI website</a>

4740. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

4741ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4742.</p>

4743<p id="_1820" tabindex="-1">

4744The following are examples of national and/or institutional clinical trials that are currently being conducted:</p>

4745<div class="pdq-content-list">

4746<ul id="_1798">

4747<li>

4748<strong>

4749<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03533582">

4750AHEP1531 (NCT03533582)</a>

4751</strong>

4752 (Cisplatin and Combination Chemotherapy in Treating Children and Young Adults with Hepatoblastoma or Liver Cancer After Surgery)<strong>

4753:</strong>

4754 <p id="_1942" tabindex="-1">

4755This is the COG's participation in a large international trial (PHITT) of treatment of all stages of hepatoblastoma and hepatocellular carcinoma in children.</p>

4756 <div class="pdq-content-list">

4757<ul id="_1940">

4758<li>

4759Very low-risk hepatoblastoma is defined as either: 1) a well-differentiated fetal mass completely resected at diagnosis, and patients receive no chemotherapy; or 2) a non&#8211;well-differentiated fetal mass or incompletely resected well-differentiated fetal mass, and patients are treated with two cycles of cisplatin chemotherapy. </li>

4760<li>

4761Low-risk hepatoblastoma is defined as a PRETEXT I to III tumor without any positive VPEFR annotation factors (venous involvement, portal involvement, extrahepatic spread, multifocality, and tumor rupture). These patients are treated with two cycles of cisplatin chemotherapy and then undergo resection (if possible), followed by randomization between two and four more cycles of cisplatin. If the tumor is unresectable, the patient receives two more cycles of cisplatin chemotherapy, and the tumor's resectability is reassessed. If it is still unresectable, patients undergo liver transplant.</li>

4762<li>

4763Intermediate-risk hepatoblastoma is defined as a PRETEXT I to III primary tumor with a positive VPEFR annotation factor but without metastasis. Patients are randomly assigned to either four 14-day cycles of cisplatin or four 21-day cycles of C5VD (cisplatin, fluorouracil, vincristine, and doxorubicin). Transplant teams are consulted early as needed. Patients in both arms then undergo resection and receive two more cycles of their assigned chemotherapy.</li>

4764<li>

4765High-risk hepatoblastoma is defined as presence of distant metastasis, AFP less than 100, or age 8 years and older. All patients are treated with three cycles of induction chemotherapy per the SIOPEL-4 trial (dose-intensive cisplatin, doxorubicin, and carboplatin). The patients aged 8 years and older or with AFP less than 100 and those whose metastases have cleared receive three cycles of carboplatin and doxorubicin. Patients with metastases that have not cleared by end of induction are randomly assigned to receive either carboplatin/doxorubicin cycles alternating with carboplatin/etoposide for six cycles or carboplatin/doxorubicin alternating with vincristine/irinotecan for six cycles.</li>

4766</ul>

4767</div>

4768</li>

4769<li>

4770<strong>

4771<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03155620">

4772APEC1621 (NCT03155620)</a>

4773</strong>

4774 (Pediatric MATCH: Targeted Therapy Directed by Genetic Testing in Treating Pediatric Patients with Relapsed or Refractory Advanced Solid Tumors, Non-Hodgkin Lymphomas, or Histiocytic Disorders)<strong>

4775:</strong>

4776 NCI-COG Pediatric Molecular Analysis for Therapeutic Choice (MATCH), referred to as Pediatric MATCH, will match targeted agents with specific molecular changes identified in a patient's tumor (refractory or recurrent). Children and adolescents aged 1 to 21 years are eligible for the trial.<p id="_1807" tabindex="-1">

4777Patients with tumors that have molecular variants addressed by treatment arms included in the trial will be offered treatment on Pediatric MATCH. Additional information can be obtained on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match">

4778NCI website</a>

4779 and <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620">

4780ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4781.</p>

4782

4783</li>

4784</ul>

4785</div>

4786</section>

5432<section id="_158">

5433<h3 id="_158_toc">

5434Incidence </h3>

5435<p id="_354" tabindex="-1">

5436The annual incidence of hepatocellular carcinoma in the United States is 0.4 cases per 1 million children between the ages of 0 and 14 years and 1.4 cases per 1 million adolescents aged 15 to 19 years.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

54371</a>

5438] Although the incidence of hepatocellular carcinoma in adults in the United States has steadily increased since the 1970s, possibly because of the increased frequency of chronic hepatitis C infection,[<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

54392</a>

5440] the incidence in children has not increased. In several Asian countries, the incidence of hepatocellular carcinoma in children is 10 times higher than in North America. The high incidence appears to be related to the incidence of perinatally acquired hepatitis B, which can be prevented in most cases by vaccination and administration of hepatitis B immune globulin to the newborn child.[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

54413</a>

5442]</p>

5443<p id="_604" tabindex="-1">

5444Fibrolamellar hepatocellular carcinoma, a subtype of hepatocellular carcinoma that is unrelated to cirrhosis, hepatitis B virus (HBV), or hepatitis C virus (HCV) infection, generally occurs in adolescents and young adults, but has been reported in infants.[<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

54454</a>

5446]</p>

5447</section>

5524<section id="_576">

5525<h4 id="_576_toc">

5526Alagille syndrome</h4>

5527<p id="_577" tabindex="-1">

5528Alagille syndrome is an autosomal dominant genetic syndrome that is usually caused by a mutation in or deletion of the <em class="gene-name">

5529JAG1</em>

5530 gene. It involves the bile ducts of the liver, as well as the heart and blood vessels in the brain and kidney. Patients develop a characteristic facies.[<a href="#cit/section_7.5">

55315</a>

5532]</p>

5533</section>

5534<section id="_515">

5535<h4 id="_515_toc">

5536Hepatitis B and hepatitis C infection</h4>

5537<p id="_516" tabindex="-1">

5538In children, hepatocellular carcinoma is associated with perinatally acquired HBV. In adults, it is associated with chronic HBV and HCV infection.[<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

55397</a>

5540-<a href="#cit/section_7.9">

55419</a>

5542] Widespread

5543hepatitis B immunization has decreased the incidence of hepatocellular

5544carcinoma in Asia.[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

55453</a>

5546] Compared with adults, the incubation period from hepatitis virus

5547infection to the genesis of hepatocellular carcinoma is extremely short in a small subset of children with perinatally acquired virus. Mutations in the <em class="gene-name">

5548MET</em>

5549 gene could be one mechanism that results in a shortened incubation period.[<a href="#cit/section_7.13">

555013</a>

5551]</p>

5552<p id="_1886" tabindex="-1">

5553 Hepatitis C infection is associated with development of cirrhosis and hepatocellular carcinoma that takes decades to develop and is generally not seen in children.[<a href="#cit/section_7.9">

55549</a>

5555] Unlike in adults, cirrhosis in children is much less commonly involved in the development of hepatocellular carcinoma and is found in only 20% to 35% of children with hepatocellular carcinoma tumors.</p>

5556</section>

5557<section id="_899">

5558<h4 id="_899_toc">

5559Nonviral liver injury</h4>

5560<p id="_900" tabindex="-1">

5561Specific types of nonviral liver injury and cirrhosis that are

5562associated with hepatocellular carcinoma in children include the following:</p>

5563<div class="pdq-content-list">

5564<ul id="_901">

5565<li>

5566<strong>

5567Tyrosinemia.</strong>

5568 Patients with tyrosinemia are regularly screened for hepatocellular carcinoma, even if they are treated with nitisinone.[<a href="#cit/section_7.12">

556912</a>

5570]

5571Nitisinone can prevent cirrhosis and decrease the incidence of hepatocellular carcinoma, especially when administered during infancy, after neonatal screening is used to diagnose tyrosinemia.[<a href="#cit/section_7.14">

557214</a>

5573] As of 2014, only a minority of state screening programs had adopted a highly recommended, new, more predictive newborn screen that is much more effective in newborn children aged 24 to 48 hours.[<a href="#cit/section_7.15">

557415</a>

5575] <p id="_903" tabindex="-1">

5576In an Iranian study, 36 children underwent liver transplant for tyrosinemia.[<a href="#cit/section_7.16">

557716</a>

5578] Twenty-two children had liver nodules greater than 10 cm, and in 20 children, the nodules were cirrhotic. Median age at transplant was 3.9 years. Five of 19 children older than 2 years had hepatocellular carcinoma, and no children younger than 2 years had hepatocellular carcinoma in the resected liver.</p>

5579</li>

5580<li>

5581<strong>

5582Aggressive familial intrahepatic cholestasis.</strong>

5583 Hepatocellular carcinoma may also arise in very young children with mutations in the <em class="gene-name">

5584ABCB11</em>

5585 gene (encodes bile salt export pump protein), which causes progressive familial intrahepatic cholestasis.[<a href="#cit/section_7.10">

558610</a>

5587] </li>

5588</ul>

5589</div>

5590</section>

5591</section>

5592<section id="_1935">

5593<h3 id="_1935_toc">

5594Genomics of Hepatocellular Carcinoma</h3>

5595<section id="_sm_CDR0000795795_751">

5596<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000795795_751_toc">

5597Molecular features of hepatocellular carcinoma</h4>

5598<p id="_sm_CDR0000795795_732" tabindex="-1">

5599Genomic findings related to hepatocellular carcinoma include the following:</p>

5600<div class="pdq-content-list">

5601<ul id="_sm_CDR0000795795_733">

5602<li>

5603One case of pediatric hepatocellular carcinoma was analyzed by whole-exome sequencing, which showed a higher mutation rate (53 variants) and the coexistence of <em class="gene-name">

5604CTNNB1</em>

5605 and <em class="gene-name">

5606NFE2L2</em>

5607 mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_7.17">

560817</a>

5609]</li>

5610<li>

5611One study investigated pediatric nonfibrolamellar hepatocellular carcinoma tumors (N = 15) using multiple analytic tools. These tumors were found to frequently carry alterations in a subset of genes that are commonly mutated in adult hepatocellular carcinoma, including <em class="gene-name">

5612CTNNB1</em>

5613 and <em class="gene-name">

5614TERT</em>

5615, but the molecular mechanisms of the mutations are different. The <em class="gene-name">

5616TP53</em>

5617 mutation was rare in this pediatric hepatocellular carcinoma cohort. Pediatric hepatocellular carcinoma that arose in the background of underlying metabolic disease had fewer mutations and a distinct molecular profile. Typical driver mutations were lacking in this group of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_7.18">

561818</a>

5619]</li>

5620<li>

5621Fibrolamellar hepatocellular carcinoma is a rare subtype of hepatocellular carcinoma observed in older children and young adults. It is characterized by an approximately 400 kB deletion on chromosome 19 that results in production of a chimeric RNA coding for a protein containing the amino-terminal domain of <em class="gene-name">

5622DNAJB1</em>

5623, a homolog of the molecular chaperone DNAJ, fused in frame with <em class="gene-name">

5624PRKACA</em>

5625, the catalytic domain of protein kinase A.[<a href="#cit/section_7.19">

562619</a>

5627]</li>

5628<li>

5629A rare, more aggressive subtype of childhood liver cancer (hepatocellular neoplasm, not otherwise specified, also termed transitional liver cell tumor) occurs in older children. It has clinical and histopathological findings of both hepatoblastoma and hepatocellular carcinoma. <p id="_sm_CDR0000795795_750" tabindex="-1">

5630<em class="gene-name">

5631TERT</em>

5632 mutations were observed in two of four transitional liver cell tumor cases tested.[<a href="#cit/section_7.20">

563320</a>

5634] <em class="gene-name">

5635TERT</em>

5636 mutations are also commonly observed in adults with hepatocellular carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.21">

563721</a>

5638]</p>

5639</li>

5640</ul>

5641</div>

5642<p id="_sm_CDR0000795795_748" tabindex="-1">

5643To date, these genetic mutations have not been used to select therapeutic agents for investigation in clinical trials.</p>

5644</section>

5645</section>

5646<section id="_596">

5647<h3 id="_596_toc">

5648Diagnosis</h3>

5649<p id="_597" tabindex="-1">

5650For more information, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_366">

5651Diagnosis</a>

5652 section in the Hepatoblastoma section.</p>

5653</section>

5654<section id="_159">

5655<h3 id="_159_toc">

5656Prognosis and Prognostic Factors</h3>

5657<section id="_2399">

5658<h4 id="_2399_toc">

5659Prognosis</h4>

5660<p id="_381" tabindex="-1">

5661The 5-year overall survival (OS) rate is 42% for children and adolescents with hepatocellular carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.22">

566222</a>

5663] The 5-year survival for patients with hepatocellular carcinoma may depend on the stage of the disease. In an intergroup chemotherapy study conducted in the 1990s, seven of eight stage I patients survived, and less than 10% of stage III and IV patients survived.[<a href="#cit/section_7.22">

566422</a>

5665,<a href="#cit/section_7.23">

566623</a>

5667] An analysis of Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program data found a 5-year OS rate of 24%, a 10-year rate of 23%, and a 20-year rate of 8% in patients aged 19 years and younger, suggesting improved outcome related to more recent treatment. In a multivariate analysis of the SEER data, surgical resection, localized tumor, and non-Hispanic ethnicity were all associated with improved outcome. Patients who had a complete surgical resection had an OS rate of 60%, compared with an OS rate of 0% for patients who had an incomplete resection.[<a href="#cit/section_7.24">

566824</a>

5669][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5670Level of evidence C1</a>

5671]</p>

5672<p id="_1861" tabindex="-1">

5673The 5-year OS rates by PRE-Treatment EXTent of disease (PRETEXT) group for patients with hepatocellular carcinoma in the SIOPEL-1 trial were found to be the following:[<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

567425</a>

5675]</p>

5676<div class="pdq-content-list">

5677<ul id="_1862">

5678<li>

567944% for patients with PRETEXT I group tumors.</li>

5680<li>

568144% for patients with PRETEXT II group tumors.</li>

5682<li>

568322% for patients with PRETEXT III group tumors.</li>

5684<li>

56858% for patients with PRETEXT IV group tumors.</li>

5686</ul>

5687</div>

5688<p id="_2425" tabindex="-1">

5689For more information about PRETEXT grouping, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_728">

5690PRETEXT and POSTTEXT Group Definitions</a>

5691 section.</p>

5692<p id="_2406" tabindex="-1">

5693<strong>

5694Hepatocellular carcinoma prognosis by Evans surgical stage.</strong>

5695 For more information, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_11">

5696Evans Surgical Staging for Childhood Liver Cancer (Historical)</a>

5697 section.</p>

5698<div class="pdq-content-list">

5699<ul id="_2407">

5700<li>

5701<strong>

5702Stage I.</strong>

5703<p id="_1864" tabindex="-1">

5704Children with stage I hepatocellular carcinoma have a

5705good outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_7.26">

570626</a>

5707]</p>

5708</li>

5709<li>

5710<strong>

5711Stage II.</strong>

5712<p id="_1866" tabindex="-1">

5713Stage II is too rarely seen to predict outcome.</p>

5714</li>

5715<li>

5716<strong>

5717Stages III and IV.</strong>

5718<p id="_1868" tabindex="-1">

5719Stages

5720III and IV are usually fatal.[<a href="#cit/section_7.23">

572123</a>

5722,<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

572325</a>

5724]</p>

5725</li>

5726</ul>

5727</div>

5728</section>

5729<section id="_2400">

5730<h4 id="_2400_toc">

5731Prognostic factors</h4>

5732<p id="_598" tabindex="-1">

5733Factors affecting prognosis include the following:</p>

5734<div class="pdq-content-list">

5735<ul id="_599">

5736<li>

5737<strong>

5738Treatment-related factors:</strong>

5739 Cure of hepatocellular carcinoma requires gross tumor resection. However, hepatocellular carcinoma is often extensively invasive or multicentric, and less than 30% of tumors are resectable. Orthotopic liver transplant has been successful in selected children with hepatocellular carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.27">

574027</a>

5741,<a href="#cit/section_7.28">

574228</a>

5743]</li>

5744<li>

5745<strong>

5746PRETEXT group:</strong>

5747 PRETEXT group (resectability) is also a prognostic factor. For more information, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_10">

5748Tumor Stratification by Imaging and Evans Surgical Staging for Childhood Liver Cancer</a>

5749 section.</li>

5750<li>

5751<strong>

5752Tumor histology:</strong>

5753 For more information, see the <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_606">

5754Histology</a>

5755 section.</li>

5756</ul>

5757</div>

5758</section>

5759</section>

5760<section id="_606">

5761<h3 id="_606_toc">

5762Histology</h3>

5763<p id="_108" tabindex="-1">

5764The cells of hepatocellular carcinoma are epithelial in appearance.

5765Hepatocellular carcinoma commonly arises in the right lobe of the liver. </p>

5766<section id="_497">

5767<h4 id="_497_toc">

5768Fibrolamellar carcinoma</h4>

5769<p id="_498" tabindex="-1">

5770 A distinctive histological variant

5771of hepatocellular carcinoma, termed fibrolamellar carcinoma, has been described in the livers of

5772older children, young adults, and, rarely, infants.[<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

57734</a>

5774,<a href="#cit/section_7.29">

577529</a>

5776] This histology is characterized by a fusion transcript created by deletion of a 400 kb section of chromosome 19, which was found in 15 of 15 tumors that were tested.[<a href="#cit/section_7.19">

577719</a>

5778] </p>

5779<p id="_607" tabindex="-1">

5780 Unlike nonfibrolamellar hepatocellular carcinoma in adults, fibrolamellar hepatocellular carcinoma in older children and adults is not clearly increasing in incidence over time.[<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

57812</a>

5782,<a href="#cit/section_7.29">

578329</a>

5784] Fibrolamellar carcinoma is not associated with cirrhosis and was previously thought to be associated with an improved prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

57852</a>

5786,<a href="#cit/section_7.29">

578729</a>

5788,<a href="#cit/section_7.30">

578930</a>

5790] The improved outcomes of patients with fibrolamellar carcinoma in older studies may be related to a higher proportion of tumors being less invasive and more resectable in the absence of cirrhosis. However, the outcomes of patients with fibrolamellar carcinoma in recent prospective studies, when compared stage to stage and PRETEXT group to PRETEXT group, are the same as the outcomes of patients with conventional hepatocellular carcinomas.[<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

579125</a>

5792,<a href="#cit/section_7.31">

579331</a>

5794]; [<a href="#cit/section_7.32">

579532</a>

5796][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5797Level of evidence C1</a>

5798] </p>

5799</section>

5800<section id="_500">

5801<h4 id="_500_toc">

5802Hepatocellular neoplasm, not otherwise specified (NOS)</h4>

5803<p id="_501" tabindex="-1">

5804Hepatocellular neoplasm, NOS, is also known as transitional liver cell tumor. This tumor, with characteristics of both hepatoblastoma and hepatocellular carcinoma, is a rare neoplasm found in older children and adolescents. It has a putative intermediate position between hepatoblasts and more mature hepatocyte-like tumor cells. The tumor cells may vary in regions of the tumor between classical hepatoblastoma and obvious hepatocellular carcinoma. In the international consensus classification, these tumors are referred to as hepatocellular neoplasm, NOS.[<a href="#cit/section_7.33">

580533</a>

5806] The tumors are usually unifocal and may have central necrosis at presentation. Response to chemotherapy has not been rigorously studied, but it is thought to be similar to that of hepatocellular carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.34">

580734</a>

5808]</p>

5809</section>

5810</section>

5811<section id="_374">

5812<h3 id="_374_toc">

5813Treatment of Hepatocellular Carcinoma</h3>

5814<p id="_750" tabindex="-1">

5815Treatment options for newly diagnosed hepatocellular carcinoma depend on the following:</p>

5816<div class="pdq-content-list">

5817<ol id="_751">

5818<li>

5819Whether the cancer is resectable at diagnosis.</li>

5820<li>

5821How the cancer responds to chemotherapy.</li>

5822<li>

5823Whether the cancer has metastasized.</li>

5824<li>

5825Whether the cancer is HBV related. </li>

5826</ol>

5827</div>

5828<section id="_291">

5829<h4 id="_291_toc">

5830Treatment options for hepatocellular carcinoma that is resectable at diagnosis</h4>

5831<p id="_697" tabindex="-1">

5832Treatment options for hepatocellular carcinoma that is resectable at diagnosis include the following:</p>

5833<div class="pdq-content-list">

5834<ol id="_698">

5835<li>

5836Complete surgical resection of the primary tumor followed by chemotherapy.</li>

5837<li>

5838Chemotherapy followed by complete surgical resection of the primary tumor.[<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

583925</a>

5840]</li>

5841<li>

5842Complete surgical resection without chemotherapy.</li>

5843</ol>

5844</div>

5845<p id="_699" tabindex="-1">

5846Surgical resection and chemotherapy are the mainstays of treatment for resectable hepatocellular carcinoma.</p>

5847<p id="_700" tabindex="-1">

5848Evidence (complete surgical resection followed by chemotherapy):</p>

5849<div class="pdq-content-list">

5850<ol id="_701">

5851<li>

5852Seven of eight patients with stage I hepatocellular carcinoma who received adjuvant cisplatin-based chemotherapy survived disease free.[<a href="#cit/section_7.23">

585323</a>

5854]</li>

5855<li>

5856In a survey of childhood liver tumors treated before the consistent use of chemotherapy, only 12 of 33 patients with hepatocellular carcinoma who had complete excision of the tumor survived.[<a href="#cit/section_7.35">

585735</a>

5858] This suggests that treatment with adjuvant chemotherapy may benefit children with completely resected hepatocellular carcinoma.</li>

5859<li>

5860 In an analysis of SEER data for children and adolescents younger than 20 years who were diagnosed between 1976 and 2009, patients who underwent a complete resection had a 5-year OS rate of 60%, and patients who did not have a complete resection had a 5-year OS rate of 0%.[<a href="#cit/section_7.24">

586124</a>

5862][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5863Level of evidence C1</a>

5864]</li>

5865</ol>

5866</div>

5867<p id="_2401" tabindex="-1">

5868Cisplatin and doxorubicin may be administered as adjuvant therapy because these agents may have activity in the treatment of hepatocellular carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

586925</a>

5870]</p>

5871<p id="_1900" tabindex="-1">

5872Evidence (complete surgical resection without chemotherapy):</p>

5873<div class="pdq-content-list">

5874<ol id="_1901">

5875<li>

5876In a single-institution retrospective report, 12 patients with stage I hepatocellular carcinoma were treated with surgery. Ten patients received no chemotherapy and two patients received a short course of chemotherapy based on oncologist preference.[<a href="#cit/section_7.36">

587736</a>

5878][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

5879Level of evidence C1</a>

5880]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5881<ul id="_1902">

5882<li>

5883All 12 patients were alive without evidence of disease at a median of 54 months.</li>

5884</ul>

5885</div>

5886</li>

5887</ol>

5888</div>

5889<p id="_702" tabindex="-1">

5890Despite improvements in surgical techniques, chemotherapy delivery, and patient supportive care in the past 20 years, clinical trials of chemotherapy have not shown improved survival rates for pediatric patients with hepatocellular carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

589125</a>

5892] The International Childhood Liver Tumors Strategy Group (SIOPEL) studies in Europe have observed no improvement in 5-year OS since 1990. The only long-term survivors were patients whose tumors were resectable at diagnosis, which was less than 30% of children entered in the study.[<a href="#cit/section_7.37">

589337</a>

5894] However, some liver transplant studies (complete resection with transplant with or without neoadjuvant chemotherapy) have shown OS rates that are superior to the SIOPEL studies.[<a href="#cit/section_7.28">

589528</a>

5896,<a href="#cit/section_7.38">

589738</a>

5898-<a href="#cit/section_7.41">

589941</a>

5900]</p>

5901</section>

5902<section id="_295">

5903<h4 id="_295_toc">

5904Treatment options for nonmetastatic hepatocellular carcinoma that is not resectable at diagnosis</h4>

5905<p id="_704" tabindex="-1">

5906Treatment options for nonmetastatic hepatocellular carcinoma that is not resectable at diagnosis include the following:</p>

5907<div class="pdq-content-list">

5908<ol id="_705">

5909<li>

5910Chemotherapy followed by reassessment of surgical resectability. If the primary tumor is resectable, complete surgical resection. </li>

5911<li>

5912Chemotherapy with or without transarterial radioembolization (TARE) followed by reassessment of surgical resectability. If the primary tumor remains unresectable: <div class="pdq-content-list">

5913<ul id="_774">

5914<li>

5915Orthotopic liver transplant.</li>

5916<li>

5917Temporizing transarterial chemoembolization (TACE) or TARE followed by complete resection or liver transplant.</li>

5918<li>

5919TACE or TARE alone.</li>

5920</ul>

5921</div>

5922</li>

5923</ol>

5924</div>

5925<p id="_703" tabindex="-1">

5926The use of neoadjuvant chemotherapy or TACE to enhance resectability or liver transplant, which may result in complete resection of tumor, is necessary for a cure.</p>

5927<p id="_706" tabindex="-1">

5928Evidence (chemotherapy followed by surgery):</p>

5929<div class="pdq-content-list">

5930<ol id="_707">

5931<li>

5932In a prospective study of 41 patients who received preoperative cisplatin/doxorubicin chemotherapy, the following was observed:[<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

593325</a>

5934] <div class="pdq-content-list">

5935<ul id="_1991">

5936<li>

5937Treatment resulted in a decrease in tumor size, with a decrease in alpha-fetoprotein (AFP) levels in about 50% of patients. </li>

5938<li>

5939The responders had a superior tumor resectability and survival, although the OS rate was 28% and only those undergoing complete resection survived.</li>

5940</ul>

5941</div>

5942</li>

5943</ol>

5944</div>

5945<p id="_709" tabindex="-1">

5946Evidence (chemotherapy, TARE, or TACE followed by reassessment of surgical resectability; treatment options, including liver transplant, for unresectable primary tumor after chemotherapy, TARE, or TACE):</p>

5947<div class="pdq-content-list">

5948<ol id="_710">

5949<li>

5950Liver transplant has been a successful therapy for children with unresectable hepatocellular carcinoma. The survival rate is about 60%, with most deaths resulting from tumor recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_7.27">

595127</a>

5952,<a href="#cit/section_7.41">

595341</a>

5954-<a href="#cit/section_7.44">

595544</a>

5956]</li>

5957<li>

5958A review of SEER data for hepatocellular carcinoma treatment in patients younger than 20 years revealed that 75% of patients underwent resection and 25% underwent liver transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_7.45">

595945</a>

5960]<div class="pdq-content-list">

5961<ul id="_1992">

5962<li>

5963The 5-year OS rate was 53.4% with resection and 85.3% with transplant, suggesting that the criteria for transplantation in hepatocellular carcinoma might be liberalized for overall patient benefit. This data has not been verified in a prospective clinical trial.</li>

5964</ul>

5965</div>

5966</li>

5967<li>

5968TACE followed by complete surgical resection of the primary tumor may be an alternative to the use of chemotherapy followed by surgical resection.<div class="pdq-content-list">

5969<ul id="_1887">

5970<li>

5971 Studies in adults in China suggest that repeated hepatic TACE before surgery may improve the outcome of subsequent hepatectomy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.46">

597246</a>

5973]</li>

5974<li>

5975A meta-analysis found seven randomized trials that compared resection alone with TACE followed by resection. There was no difference in the 3-year event-free survival (EFS) and OS between the two groups, but the 5-year EFS and OS favored TACE followed by resection.[<a href="#cit/section_7.47">

597647</a>

5977]</li>

5978</ul>

5979</div>

5980</li>

5981<li>

5982TARE has been used in the treatment of adult patients with hepatocellular carcinoma for some time. In a small number of patients, TARE has provided both a palliative benefit and a possible bridge to liver transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_7.48">

598348</a>

5984,<a href="#cit/section_7.49">

598549</a>

5986]</li>

5987</ol>

5988</div>

5989<p id="_711" tabindex="-1">

5990If the primary tumor is not resectable after chemotherapy and the patient is not a transplant candidate, alternative treatment approaches used in adults include the following:</p>

5991<div class="pdq-content-list">

5992<ul id="_712">

5993<li>

5994Sorafenib.</li>

5995<li>

5996TACE or TARE.</li>

5997<li>

5998Cryosurgery.</li>

5999<li>

6000Intratumoral injection of alcohol.</li>

6001<li>

6002Radiation therapy.</li>

6003</ul>

6004</div>

6005<p id="_753" tabindex="-1">

6006There are limited data on the use of these alternative treatment approaches in children.</p>

6007<p id="_754" tabindex="-1">

6008Limited data from a European pilot study suggest that sorafenib was well tolerated in 12 children and adolescents with newly diagnosed advanced hepatocellular carcinoma when given in combination with standard chemotherapy of cisplatin and doxorubicin.[<a href="#cit/section_7.50">

600950</a>

6010] Additional study is needed to define its role in the treatment of children with hepatocellular carcinoma.</p>

6011<p id="_714" tabindex="-1">

6012Cryosurgery, intratumoral injection of alcohol, and radiofrequency ablation can successfully treat small (&lt;5 cm) tumors in adults with cirrhotic livers.[<a href="#cit/section_7.46">

601346</a>

6014,<a href="#cit/section_7.51">

601551</a>

6016,<a href="#cit/section_7.52">

601752</a>

6018] Some local approaches such as cryosurgery, radiofrequency ablation, and TACE, which suppress hepatocellular carcinoma tumor progression, are used as bridging therapy in adults to delay tumor growth while on a waiting list for cadaveric liver transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_7.53">

601953</a>

6020] In a pediatric study of eight patients with hepatocellular carcinoma, two patients died of progressive disease without transplant. Treatment with TACE stabilized disease in six patients, for a mean of 141 days to reach transplant.[<a href="#cit/section_7.54">

602154</a>

6022][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

6023Level of evidence C1</a>

6024] Five patients were alive at the end of the observation period, and one patient died of disease. For more information, see <a href="/types/liver/hp/adult-liver-treatment-pdq">

6025Primary Liver Cancer

6026Treatment</a>

6027.</p>

6028</section>

6029<section id="_308">

6030<h4 id="_308_toc">

6031Treatment options for hepatocellular carcinoma with metastases at diagnosis</h4>

6032<p id="_1888" tabindex="-1">

6033No specific treatment has proven effective for metastatic hepatocellular carcinoma in the pediatric age group.</p>

6034<p id="_313" tabindex="-1">

6035In two prospective trials, cisplatin plus either vincristine/fluorouracil or continuous-infusion doxorubicin was ineffective in adequately treating 25 patients with metastatic hepatocellular carcinoma.[<a href="#cit/section_7.23">

603623</a>

6037,<a href="#cit/section_7.25">

603825</a>

6039] Occasional patients may transiently benefit from treatment with cisplatin/doxorubicin therapy, especially if the localized hepatic tumor shrinks adequately enough to allow resection of disease and the metastatic disease disappears or becomes resectable.</p>

6040</section>

6041<section id="_534">

6042<h4 id="_534_toc">

6043Treatment options for HBV-related hepatocellular carcinoma

6044</h4>

6045<p id="_535" tabindex="-1">

6046Although HBV-related hepatocellular carcinoma is not common in children in the United States, nucleotide/nucleoside analog HBV inhibitor treatment improves postoperative prognosis in children and adults treated in China.[<a href="#cit/section_7.55">

604755</a>

6048] </p>

6049<p id="_755" tabindex="-1">

6050Treatment options for HBV-related hepatocellular carcinoma include the following:</p>

6051<div class="pdq-content-list">

6052<ol id="_756">

6053<li>

6054Antiviral therapy.</li>

6055</ol>

6056</div>

6057<p id="_640" tabindex="-1">

6058Evidence (antiviral therapy):</p>

6059<div class="pdq-content-list">

6060<ol id="_641">

6061<li>

6062In a randomized controlled trial, 163 patients post&#8211;radical hepatectomy were evaluated for response to one of three antiviral treatments.[<a href="#cit/section_7.55">

606355</a>

6064]<div class="pdq-content-list">

6065<ul id="_752">

6066<li>

6067Antiviral treatment significantly decreased hepatocellular carcinoma recurrence, with a hazard ratio (HR) of 0.48 (95% confidence interval [CI], 0.32&#8211;0.70), and

6068hepatocellular carcinoma&#8211;related death, with an HR of 0.26 (95% CI, 0.14&#8211;0.50), in multivariate Cox analyses.</li>

6069<li>

6070Patients who received antiviral treatment had

6071significantly decreased early recurrence (HR, 0.41; 95% CI, 0.27&#8211;0.62) and improved liver function

60726 months after surgery than did the control patients (<em>

6073P</em>

6074 &lt; .001).</li>

6075</ul>

6076</div>

6077</li>

6078</ol>

6079</div>

6080</section>

6081</section>

6082<section id="_321">

6083<h3 id="_321_toc">

6084Treatment of Progressive or Recurrent Hepatocellular Carcinoma</h3>

6085<p id="_322" tabindex="-1">

6086The prognosis for a patient with recurrent or progressive hepatocellular

6087carcinoma is extremely poor.[<a href="#cit/section_7.56">

608856</a>

6089] </p>

6090<p id="_684" tabindex="-1">

6091Treatment options for progressive or recurrent hepatocellular carcinoma include the following:</p>

6092<div class="pdq-content-list">

6093<ol id="_685">

6094<li>

6095Chemoembolization temporization before transplant or immediate liver transplant, for those with isolated recurrence in the liver.[<a href="#cit/section_7.27">

609627</a>

6097,<a href="#cit/section_7.41">

609841</a>

6099,<a href="#cit/section_7.42">

610042</a>

6101,<a href="#cit/section_7.57">

610257</a>

6103]

6104</li>

6105<li>

6106Phase I and phase II clinical trials may be appropriate and

6107should be considered.</li>

6108</ol>

6109</div>

6110<p id="_2402" tabindex="-1">

6111Treatment with sorafenib has resulted in improved progression-free survival in adults with advanced hepatocellular carcinoma. For adult patients who received sorafenib, the median survival and time to radiologic progression were about 3 months longer than for patients who received a placebo.[<a href="#cit/section_7.58">

611258</a>

6113] </p>

6114</section>

6115<section id="_1799">

6116<h3 id="_1799_toc">

6117Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation for Hepatocellular Carcinoma</h3>

6118<p id="_1800" tabindex="-1">

6119Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

6120NCI website</a>

6121. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

6122ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

6123.</p>

6124<p id="_1821" tabindex="-1">

6125The following are examples of national and/or institutional clinical trials that are currently being conducted:</p>

6126<div class="pdq-content-list">

6127<ul id="_1801">

6128<li>

6129<strong>

6130<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03533582">

6131AHEP1531 (NCT03533582)</a>

6132</strong>

6133 (Cisplatin and Combination Chemotherapy in Treating Children and Young Adults with Hepatoblastoma or Liver Cancer After Surgery)<strong>

6134:</strong>

6135 <p id="_1960" tabindex="-1">

6136This is the COG's participation in a large international trial (Pediatric Hepatic Malignancy International Therapeutic Trial [PHITT]) of treatment of all stages of hepatoblastoma and hepatocellular carcinoma in children.</p>

6137 <div class="pdq-content-list">

6138<ul id="_1961">

6139<li>

6140Hepatocellular carcinoma that is potentially resectable is treated with complete resection without any chemotherapy if the mass appears to derive from underlying liver disease. If the hepatocellular carcinoma appears to be de novo without underlying disease, it is treated with resection followed by four cycles of cisplatin/doxorubicin.</li>

6141<li>

6142Patients with hepatocellular carcinoma that is metastatic or appears unresectable at diagnosis undergo consultation with interventional radiology and liver transplant services. Patients are then randomly assigned to receive either cisplatin/doxorubicin/sorafenib for three 21-day cycles or cisplatin/doxorubicin/sorafenib alternating with gemcitabine/oxaliplatin/sorafenib for four 14-day cycles. Responding patients continue chemotherapy for the same number of cycles (three or four). They receive the same chemotherapy regimen to which they were originally assigned.</li>

6143</ul>

6144</div>

6145</li>

6146<li>

6147<strong>

6148<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03155620">

6149APEC1621 (NCT03155620)</a>

6150</strong>

6151 (Pediatric MATCH: Targeted Therapy Directed by Genetic Testing in Treating Pediatric Patients with Relapsed or Refractory Advanced Solid Tumors, Non-Hodgkin Lymphomas, or Histiocytic Disorders)<strong>

6152:</strong>

6153 NCI-COG Pediatric Molecular Analysis for Therapeutic Choice (MATCH), referred to as Pediatric MATCH, will match targeted agents with specific molecular changes identified in a patient's tumor (refractory or recurrent). Children and adolescents aged 1 to 21 years are eligible for the trial.<p id="_2416" tabindex="-1">

6154Patients with tumors that have molecular variants addressed by treatment arms included in the trial will be offered treatment on Pediatric MATCH. Additional information can be obtained on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match">

6155NCI website</a>

6156 and <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620">

6157ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

6158.</p>

6159

6160</li>

6161</ul>

6162</div>

6163</section>

6406 <h2>

6407Undifferentiated Embryonal Sarcoma of the Liver</h2>

6408

6409 <div id="_section_355" class="pdq-sections">

6440<section id="_629">

6441<h3 id="_629_toc">

6442Incidence</h3>

6443<p id="_630" tabindex="-1">

6444Undifferentiated embryonal sarcoma of the liver (UESL) is a distinct clinical and pathological entity and accounts for 2% to 15% of pediatric hepatic malignancies.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

64451</a>

6446]</p>

6447</section>

6448<section id="_631">

6449<h3 id="_631_toc">

6450Diagnosis</h3>

6451<p id="_165" tabindex="-1">

6452UESL presents as an abdominal mass, often with pain or malaise, usually between the ages of 5 and 10 years. Widespread infiltration throughout the liver and pulmonary metastasis is common. It may appear solid or cystic on imaging, frequently with central necrosis. Undifferentiated sarcomas, like small cell undifferentiated hepatoblastomas, should be examined for loss of SMARCB1 expression by immunohistochemistry to help rule out rhabdoid tumor of the liver.</p>

6453<p id="_166" tabindex="-1">

6454It is important to make the diagnostic distinction between UESL and biliary tract rhabdomyosarcoma because they share some common clinical and pathological features, but treatment differs between the two, as shown in <a href="/types/liver/hp/child-liver-treatment-pdq#_344">

6455Table 8</a>

6456.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

64571</a>

6458] For more information, see <a href="/types/soft-tissue-sarcoma/hp/rhabdomyosarcoma-treatment-pdq">

6459Childhood Rhabdomyosarcoma Treatment</a>

6460.</p>

6461<table id="_344" class="table-default expandable-container">

6462<caption>
